+0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/OAWd7aC_8D5YhE9PnDhutC9jOXM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/OVNBLST7XREITK6E42MLHE76DA.jpg","linkMd5":"94a388c7b1e3df314f4fb5908e968ca4","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn4@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-06-35-191_86a46092dcd0b5a3.webp","destWidth":940,"destHeight":625,"sourceBytes":59860,"destBytes":41100,"author":"PopSci Commerce Team","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/OAWd7aC_8D5YhE9PnDhutC9jOXM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/OVNBLST7XREITK6E42MLHE76DA.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn4@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-06-35-191_86a46092dcd0b5a3.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/jX7Z7OmUMvxeStr6mvn3gkAKQ-s=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/R3PX72XBOJBYHHEI4NRD62JIB4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn48@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-01-955_c8a3f95f9dcd754b.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/MzDFRN6m8Z9QlYYqR2laKigikFM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/PGNNTDG73FELTFMYRWZS26ZMDQ.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn2@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-07-01-925_975ce4048fe8098b.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/IQuEbAnqcDRpXmwjkIc5I-FTTlM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/LT34OB7FCNA6LEAIDFGR5R3BLM.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn15@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-03-382_9f1726329cf55a09.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/GDmQ2XKKxFX5TkSv1KU3vQUthUg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/F3VF32DU5RCMFFSEYN5MZFD5LI.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn63@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-02-033_6328ccc4fb173f1d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394630},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Learn how to add closed captions to video calls, Netflix, and more","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/closed-captions-subtitles-on-everything/","description":"<img alt=\"It's a complete brain workout—you listen and read at the same time.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/v80npt3xCjkI7rvpDUXrqZQuJXc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/Z4DLQ2BSIVFCTCPBJ5BNRJQ6GA.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>It's a complete brain workout—you listen and read at the same time. (Bruno Gomiero / Unsplash/)</caption><p>People keep finding more uses for closed captions. They’re good for making content more inclusive and watching films in foreign languages, but they also allow you to enjoy videos and video calls in quiet environments, skim through clips more quickly, and even focus more on what you’re watching.</p><p>With more users requiring subtitles, the technology behind them is constantly getting better. <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/phone-calls-from-any-device/\" target=\"_blank\">Google Duo</a>, for example, is the latest app to test automatic closed captions, putting text on recorded video calls via the same artificial intelligence that powers the Google Assistant and the <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/set-up-voice-dictation/\" target=\"_blank\">voice typing feature on Google Docs</a>.</p><p>Whatever phone you use, whichever apps are your favorites, you probably already have access to closed captioning—whether thanks to content creators or AI. It’s just a matter of knowing how to activate them.</p><p>On video streaming apps</p><p>Just about every video streaming app you’ll come across will have an option for closed captions. On Netflix, for example, look for the speech bubble icon. It may actually have a label saying “<b>Audio &#38; Subtitles</b>,” depending on your device. To change font and size, go to your <a href=\"https://www.netflix.com/YourAccount\" target=\"_blank\">Netflix account page</a> on the web, then click your profile name and <b>Change</b> next to <b>Subtitle appearance</b>.</p><p>For YouTube, click the <b>cog icon</b> (on the web) or tap the <b>three dots</b> in the top right corner (on mobile) to find closed captions. You’ll get a list of languages to pick from, either provided by the video uploader or automatically generated by YouTube’s speech recognition AI—which may or may not be particularly accurate. On the web, you can click the <b>Options</b> link on the subtitles list to change the appearance of the subtitle text.</p><p>If you’re watching Hulu, you’ll need to find the <b>cog icon</b> on the playback window, and then tap or click it. Choose an option under <b>Subtitles &#38; Captions</b> (on mobile) or click <b>Subtitles &#38; Audio</b> (on the web) to get the text up on screen. Again, you’ll have more customization options to pick from when you’re watching online—follow the <b>Settings</b> link from the <b>Subtitles &#38; Audio</b> menu to find them.</p><p>Those are three of the biggest video players in the business, but other video apps have similar options, and they shouldn’t be too difficult to find within the playback interface. These settings are typically available on smart TV apps as well, though you might need to do a bit more digging to find them.</p><p>On video call apps</p><img alt=\"Video calls can be difficult when you're hard of hearing. Closed captions can help.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/TOl9Xp4H7rBA7Mi312ZYrq-GXH0=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/HV7B6P7ZSNB6XCC7POMVFLKHIQ.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>Video calls can be difficult when you're hard of hearing. Closed captions can help. (Andrea Piacquadio / Unsplash/)</caption><p>Video calls always happen in real time, so people can’t bundle subtitle files with their footage. This means that if you want captions, you’ll have to rely on the smart AI-powered voice recognition systems built into your video-calling app of choice. This technology has been around for a while, but apps have only recently begun using it for this purpose. Still, you can find the feature in a couple of them.</p><p>Leading the way here is Skype. You can turn closed captions on for all video calls by opening <b>Settings</b> (tap your avatar on mobile or the three dots by your avatar on the desktop), and then selecting <b>Calling</b> and <b>Call subtitles</b>. By default, Skype will also caption what you say on the call, which can be a little distracting, but you can opt to have subtitles only on video feeds from other participants in the chat. You can also specify a particular language that you want captioning in.</p><p>Other video calling apps have yet to take the leap in terms of real time transcription, though it is possible on Pixel phones (more on that shortly). Google Duo is taking it slow by testing automatic subtitles on Android and iOS, but only for previously recorded video and audio messages.</p><p>If you use Duo and want your recorded messages to have captions, open up <b>Settings</b> (via the three dots in the top right corner of the main screen), then choose <b>Message settings</b> and enable <b>Captions for messages</b>. At the time of writing, this feature hasn’t been completely rolled out, so be patient if you don’t see it.</p><p>On Android devices</p><img alt=\"For when you're watching YouTube on a break but don't want to bother everyone else.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/ujyqWiYLSkReQ8wSrIUvkvOwinY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/W2KK5JM2QNC3FCVHBBIOOO32QY.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>For when you're watching YouTube on a break but don't want to bother everyone else. (Ketut Subiyanto / Pexels/)</caption><p>Android has system-wide closed captioning settings that you can find by going to <b>Settings</b> and choosing <b>Accessibility</b>, followed by <b>Caption</b> preferences. You can also change the text size and color here. Keep in mind that some apps may not support these particular settings or closed captions at all, though you might be able to find caption settings inside unsupported apps.</p><p>Google is also experimenting with a feature called Live Caption, which adds real-time subtitles on top of any video or audio in any Android app. Originally it was only available for the <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/technology/google-pixel-4a/\" target=\"_blank\">Google Pixel phones</a> (from the Pixel 2 up), but the company says it plans to make the feature available to phones from other manufacturers—it just added Samsung’s Galaxy S20 to the list.</p><p>On a Pixel phone, turn Live Caption on by opening <b>Android Settings</b>, selecting <b>Accessibility</b>, and then <b>Live Caption</b>. In the same place, you can also choose to show or hide profanity and sound labels (for sounds like laughter and applause). Finally, you can opt to show a Live Caption shortcut next to the pop-up bar that appears when you press one of the volume buttons on your phone.</p><p>The next time any app plays audio or video, you should see closed captions appear in real time. Tap and drag the captions to move them, or double-tap them to increase or decrease their size.</p><p>On iOS devices</p><p>Apple hasn’t yet announced anything similar to Live Caption for iOS, but you can still enable captions for recorded content across the operating system. From <b>Settings</b>, pick <b>Accessibility</b>, then <b>Subtitles &#38; Captioning</b>;<b> </b>the next screen will let you switch captions on or off, and change their visual appearance.</p><p>As on Android, this will only work for apps that recognize the accessibility setting in iOS, but quite a number do—Amazon Prime Video and the default TV video app are two that will turn on captions in line with the general iOS settings. If you only want to turn this feature on while watching on the TV app, you can activate it by tapping the subtitles icon (a square speech bubble) on the playback screen.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 28 Aug 2020 16:00:00 brain teasers like these keep your mind sharp. (Pixabay/)</caption><p><i>We know you are bored at home right now—we are too. </i><a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/head-trip/\"><i>Here </i></a><i>are some puzzles and brainteasers to challenge your family and friends with, either in person or over video chat.</i></p><p><b>QUESTION ONE:</b></p><p>This party of locals is made up of two classes of people: warriors and lords. They are as much different as they are the same. The crucial thing you need to know about them is that warriors always tell the truth, and lords always lie. There are no exceptions to this rule.</p><p>Two people stand guard next to each other, watching the area. One wears green and the other red. A sign tells you that you can pass if you can correctly identify which type of citizen each of them is: Are they a warrior or a lord?</p><p>The one in green says: “There’s at least one lord standing before you.” Simple enough, but you can glean much information from that sentence. What type of islanders are they?</p><p><details>\n<summary>Click here for the answer (Spoiler!)</summary>\n<ol>\n        Green is a warrior, red is a lord. This remains true if the one in green is a warrior or a lord. But, if green were a lord, they’d be lying. So, green can only be a warrior, making red a lord.\n    </ol>\n</details></p><p><b>QUESTION TWO:</b></p><p>You answer correctly and continue your trek. Soon, you reach a fork in the road and another pair of locals standing guard, waiting for you to arrive.</p><p>This time, you know for sure that one of them is a lord and one is a warrior. And, as luck would have it, they know where your hotel is. However, you don’t know which is which. To find out which direction leads you back to your residence, you are allotted just one question for them to answer. What question do you ask? <i>(Hint: Decide on a query that both a warrior and a lord would have to provide the same answer to.)</i></p><p><details>\n<summary>Click here for the answer (Spoiler!)</summary>\n<ol>\n        Which direction would the other person tell me to go to get me in the right place? If the person you ask is a warrior, the other must be a lord. The lord would lie and say the opposite of where to go. If the person were a lord, the other would be\na warrior bearing the truth—but the lord would lie about what the warrior says. In either case, you would get the opposite of the correct direction.\n    </ol>\n</details></p><p><i>This article was originally published in the Winter 2018 </i><a href=\"https://bonnier.arcpublishing.com/tags/winter-2018\"><i>Danger</i></a><i> issue of</i> Popular Science.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Aug 2020 12:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/lBiRRb6nUGC2dkXlwFJBg2uE-_k=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/LBYZN5D365BX3JK45PDCZRDOQY.jpg","linkMd5":"22b3568feda1a190e5b2e50d72be17b6","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn84@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-06-35-089_6c9ed99a63cbc6ae.webp","destWidth":1707,"destHeight":1280,"sourceBytes":310781,"destBytes":287732,"author":"Claire Maldarelli","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/lBiRRb6nUGC2dkXlwFJBg2uE-_k=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/LBYZN5D365BX3JK45PDCZRDOQY.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn84@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-06-35-089_6c9ed99a63cbc6ae.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394632},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Twinkle lights that instantly cheer up your home","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/shop/twinkle-lights-for-your-home/","description":"<img alt=\"Brighten up your space.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"625\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/eyF-0gePLgMp_B3Ghn_Sq3zOaWc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/FXG677UZNZDU7KJXOFHFXYF47E.jpg\" width=\"938\"/><br/><caption>Brighten up your space. +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/eyF-0gePLgMp_B3Ghn_Sq3zOaWc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/FXG677UZNZDU7KJXOFHFXYF47E.jpg","linkMd5":"068c96740af07a3c4a6c206744d4ef74","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn92@2020_3/2020/08/31/05-06-34-935_e5423b984be501f4.webp","destWidth":938,"destHeight":625,"sourceBytes":31165,"destBytes":17726,"author":"PopSci Team","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/eyF-0gePLgMp_B3Ghn_Sq3zOaWc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/FXG677UZNZDU7KJXOFHFXYF47E.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn92@2020_3/2020/08/31/05-06-34-935_e5423b984be501f4.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/lqUrjow98H6BQQg1TOrD-tqvOk4=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/KO4LPPOJ5FCERHUKWG4JUW3ASQ.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn62@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-07-01-976_c2739199a948eba1.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/zOjCKOkUvLDsB5RNfSqRXJVQxag=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/77ZA2NCZMRBJTDLRPJ3IIDHT24.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn16@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-07-02-621_5f8f653cc976206c.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LBQ2IPYfxza-wLztRPIsm7XOQrU=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/VE3PZIL2FVHWLHQ65VDYGXJJEU.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn24@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-03-259_a59dbd3760bf5e64.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/C00eJc_AJNdjEFh67mBzwcrDfJY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/VGKPHN5VY5CSLPCYMRHBAYBFWI.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn95@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-07-01-992_bd32bf6f2cceded6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394625},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"How to help your pet with separation anxiety","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/animals/pet-separation-anxiety-tips/","description":"<img alt=\"What do you mean you have to go back to the office?!\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1280\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/yvqL7TQsYdEAkF6iJohvUbmKl0o=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/C7FNFNQUTVEVXI5T6R5EIPFIZE.jpg\" width=\"1920\"/><br/><caption>What do you mean you have to go back to the office?! (Pexels/)</caption><p><a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/tags/pet-psychic/\"><img alt=\"Pet Psychic Banner\" src=\"https://cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/COUYOTHJ5JGLXJJFJA7ZS7ZQ4Y.jpg\" style=\"width:100%\"/></a></p><p><i>Ever wish you could peer into your cat, dog, skink, or betta fish’s brain? It would give you a far better perspective of the world</i>—<i>or at least help you be a smarter pet parent. We’re here to demystify your animals (to some extent), while also shedding advice on how you can best thrive together. Welcome to Pet Psychic.</i></p><p>For the past couple of months, our pets have become our coworkers, confidants, and constant companions. With social distancing still going strong in many states, we’ve been spending a lot of time inside on our couch with our pets curled up next to us, providing adorable distractions all day long.</p><p>For better or for worse, though, <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/health/social-distancing-covid-19-spread/\" target=\"_blank\">governments have started opening up communities</a> across the country. Soon, we’ll be headed back to our offices, and our pups, kitties, and other animals will no longer have our undivided attention day in and out.</p><p>What do we do to start getting our fluffy, feathered, or scaly buddies ready for more and more alone time? Here are a few tips from animal experts on readjusting to schedules that don’t include 24/7 cuddles.</p><p>Look for warning signs</p><p>You’ve probably heard about pet separation anxiety, but what does it actually look like? Some signs would be your dog or cat exhibiting destructive behaviors after you leave home, like pacing, vocalizing, trembling, or even relieving itself on the carpet.</p><p>Separation anxiety isn’t the only kind of stress that a pet might go through as you get busy again. General anxiety due to change is also common, says Rebecca Ruch-Gallie, a veterinarian at Colorado State University’s veterinary teaching hospital: Pacing, lip licking, salivating, and a flat or sunken body posture can all be clues.</p><p>Your furry buddies might not express these behaviors while you’re around, but you can keep a watchful eye on them with a well-placed camera. If you spot something surprising on the feed, share the clip with your vet.</p><p>“We’ve been able to diagnose many pets from a video that just bark a little bit right when their owners have left, and then they just do not settle,” says Margaret Gruen, a professor of behavioral medicine at NC State University’s vet school.</p><p>It’s especially important to look out for these behaviors if you’ve just adopted a shelter animal. One of the most positive things to come out of shutdowns is that more and more <a href=\"https://www.wired.com/story/coronavirus-pet-adoption-boom/\">people have opened their hearts and homes to new furry friends.</a> But shelter animals are more likely to have anxiety, period, says Sara Bennett, a professor of behavioral medicine at NC State University’s vet school. If they’ve entered your life in a time where you can shower love on them all day and things suddenly change, you’ll want to be extra vigilant in watching for symptoms of stress.</p><p>It’s also super important to remember that whether it’s a destroyed couch or a pile of poop on the floor, your pet isn’t trying to get back at you for leaving them, says Leanne Lilley, a vet and professor of behavioral medicine at Ohio State. Your kitty or pup probably unsafe or panicked, and that’s why they are acting out. So, try your best not to get angry with them, no matter how irritating they can be.</p><p>“Vaccinate” your pet against missing you</p><p>Since the shutdowns began, many of our schedules have gone to the wayside. We’ve traded our work clothes for all-day sweatpants, and happy hour starts the second the laptop closes. That’s all fine and dandy, but a lack of a schedule can be confusing for our animals, especially when they usually have free reign of the house from 9 to 5 every day.</p><p>Some ways to start getting your pets back into the swing of things is by “vaccinating” them to being attached to your hip, says Katherine Houpt, an animal behavior expert and veterinarian at Cornell. With a dog, use a sit and stay command and then step away to see if they can resist chasing you down. If they obey and seem comfortable, there’s a good chance your pup will survive the day without you.</p><p>Another way to ease your pet into the situation is by stepping out your front door for a few minutes and then coming back in. If you keep upping the time of the experiment, your dog or cat will eventually realize that no matter how long you leave for, you’re always going to come back and be excited to see them.</p><p>You can also help get your pets back on schedule by running errands around when you’d leave the house for work, instead of some random point in the day. If you’re generally out of the house for the day by 9 a.m., let’s say, try and make your grocery run then (even if that means getting out of your pajamas before noon).</p><p>Make sure you’re not the only source of fun</p><p>We love our pets a whole lot—but we we have other relationships and commitments in our lives. For our pets, on the other hand, they pretty much just have us. They aren’t texting their litter mates to catch up and swiping around on the furry equivalent of Bumble. No pressure, but you’re pretty much your cat or dog’s world.</p><p>That makes leaving them behind to head to the office all the rougher. Luckily, dogs don’t need much to be entertained. Fill up <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/best-dog-supplies-toys/\" target=\"_blank\">a kong toy or use a puzzle feeder</a> to keep the entertainment going while you’re outside of the home. A timed kibble dispenser can also help your pet learn that you aren’t the patron saint of food.</p><p>“You become not so much the sole caretaker in the world,” Ruch-Gallie says. She also suggests leaving around special toys and snacks for when you leave, so that your cat or dog associates something positive with that alone time.</p><p>Keep up the fun things you did during shutdowns</p><p>If you’ve been taking your dog on longer, more frequent romps in the past few months, you’re not alone. It’s become a highlight in many pet owners’—and pets’—days.</p><p>Continue this new tradition of leisurely strolls filled with sniffing, socializing, and observing as life picks up again. Not only is getting out and about good for your health, it also does wonders for your dog’s welfare, Gruen says.</p><p>In this time of getting to know your animal better, you might also realize that you don’t need to crate them all day when you’re at work, Gruen says. Let them roam around in a room with some kind of monitoring system in place. “Maybe we can make some changes for the better,” Gruen adds.</p><p>If nothing else, the pandemic has likely showed you how your pet reacts to your presence. Don’t forget how much your animal loves you and is excited to share time with you, whether it’s all day or after work for a different kind of happy hour.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Aug 2020 15:58:30 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/yvqL7TQsYdEAkF6iJohvUbmKl0o=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/C7FNFNQUTVEVXI5T6R5EIPFIZE.jpg","linkMd5":"f1267eb82becef47bbe0afd6eb3eead0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn2@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-06-35-479_040ddb100d10d7e5.webp","destWidth":1920,"destHeight":1280,"sourceBytes":173765,"destBytes":90624,"author":"Sara Kiley Watson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/yvqL7TQsYdEAkF6iJohvUbmKl0o=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/C7FNFNQUTVEVXI5T6R5EIPFIZE.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn2@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-06-35-479_040ddb100d10d7e5.webp","https://cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/COUYOTHJ5JGLXJJFJA7ZS7ZQ4Y.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn31@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-03-396_dd3a5be32b6985b7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394632},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"The quest to snare—and save—the world’s largest owl","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/animals/owls-facts-book-russia/","description":"<img alt=\"With a wingspan of 5 to 6 feet and a home range in Japan and the Russian Far East, the Blakiston's fish owl is one of the most secretive birds known to conservationists.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1333\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LRg-pKPKQcvaQWYUPeCkjgv5LsQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/YB5ZRCR4KBG45HNF6Z6LSDYE4A.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>With a wingspan of 5 to 6 feet and a home range in Japan and the Russian Far East, the Blakiston's fish owl is one of the most secretive birds known to conservationists. (Sergey Gafitski/)</caption><p><i>Excerpted from </i><a href=\"https://us.macmillan.com/books/9780374228484\" target=\"_blank\">Owls of the Eastern Ice</a><i> by Jonathan C. Slaght. Published by Farrar, Straus and Giroux. Copyright © 2020 by Jonathan C. Slaght. All rights reserved.</i></p><p><b>The fall traps did not pan out.</b> Either the resident fish owls were not interested in the frozen marine fish we offered as lures or the birds were unwilling to walk under the suspicious netted domes to investigate them. At about two o’clock one morning, a few days after the blizzard ended, Sergey and I sped three kilometers on the snowmobile in response to a beeping trap transmitter, only to discover a false alarm: ice had caused the net to sag and this tugged on the string that activated the beacon. Sergey, tired and frustrated and cold, kicked at the frame, breaking it, then threw the remains into the forest. Thus concluded the fall trap experiment.</p><p>The capture learning curve was steep. There were multiple nuances specific to each trap and to each capture site. Since late February, we’d had a few very near misses. When we started out the season, we thought that four owl captures seemed like a reasonable target, but I was ready to backtrack on that goal with the realization that simply learning how to safely and efficiently catch these birds would be success enough for me for this year. If we had one or two captures to show for it at the end of the season, after all these failures, I would be satisfied. We were well past the midway point of the field season; if the weather held, we’d have three, possibly four, weeks left before the capture window closed. After that, spring would bring unstable ice, rising waters, and unsuitable conditions to trap fish owls.</p><p>The pattern of no owls, poor sleep, second-guessing, and general stagnation continued on for more than a week. I felt trapped, more so knowing that we really were trapped. Even if we wanted to throw up our hands, leave, and start fresh as we had when we left Serebryanka, we could not: our truck was still stuck in the snow a kilometer and a half away. I tried changing my outlook. We’d still made some progress this year, even if we hadn’t caught any owls. It had been arrogant of me to think we could stroll up to some of the least-studied birds in Northeast Asia and assume they’d hand us their secrets.</p><p><br/></p><p><br/></p><p><b>It was right around this time, </b>when I’d come to terms with our failures, that we caught our first owl. Anatoliy slapped me on the shoulder and told me he knew it all along—all I had needed to do was change my attitude. But in reality, we’d improved our trap. Up until this capture, we’d placed our noose carpets along the riverbank in areas we hoped the owls would land, which was inefficient. Our modification, something novel enough that we were able to later publish a description of it in a scientific journal, coaxed the owls to land where we wanted them to. We created a prey enclosure: an open-top mesh box about a meter long and thirteen centimeters tall, constructed from material left over from our noose carpets. We placed the box in shallow water no more than ten centimeters deep, sprinkled the bottom with pebbles so that from above it looked like any other stretch of river, and then filled it with as many fish as we could catch—usually fifteen or twenty salmon smolt. Then we set a single noose carpet on the closest part of the riverbank. The owl would see the fish, approach for a closer look, and get caught.</p><img alt=\"&#60;i&#62;Owls of the Eastern Ice&#60;/i&#62; by Jonathan Slaght.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1000\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/8RckKFtk67jcakcXUe5rZyavv3k=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/UM7Z37RRIBA3PC5LE2EUHLYVM4.jpg\" width=\"651\"/><br/><caption>&#60;i&#62;Owls of the Eastern Ice&#60;/i&#62; by Jonathan Slaght. (Farrar, Straus, and Giroux/)</caption><p>Masu salmon, the species most common in these rivers at this time of year, are among the smallest of all salmon. Full-sized individuals reach about half a meter in length and weigh about two kilograms, or more than half the weight of an adult fish owl. The masu have the most constricted range of any Pacific salmon, largely confined to the Sea of Japan, around the island of Sakhalin, and in western Kamchatka. Like many salmon, juvenile masu spend several years in freshwater systems before migrating to the sea, and the coastal rivers of Primorye are full of these pencil-length fish. As a result, this abundant species is a critical resource for fish owls in winter. Masu are also an important food source for local villagers, who can catch scores of them in a leisurely day of ice fishing. There is a misconception among locals that the small masu found in winter—which they call pestrushka— are a different species entirely from the larger fish—called sima— that come in summer to spawn. This complicates management of this species, as the same person who recognizes the commercial and ecological importance of sima may view the pestrushka as a common species that can be exploited.</p><p>The second night after we arranged this trap configuration, the male fish owl of the Faata River pair approached the enclosure and ate half the salmon inside before stumbling onto the noose carpet on the bank and engaging the trap transmitter. We were eating dinner by kerosene lantern light, as the hydroelectric plant no longer generated electricity, when it sounded. Despite nothing but false alarms so far, we treated every trigger with dead seriousness. Sergey and I stared for a second at the receiver and its regular, confident beeps, then locked eyes and flew out the door in a tumult of down jackets, hip waders, and uninhibited urgency.</p><p>We approached the trap, a few hundred meters away, on skis. Up ahead I saw Sergey’s spotlight reveal a fish owl sitting on the bank, watching us. Like one of Jim Henson’s darker creations, this was a goblin bird with mottled brown feathers puffed out, back hunched, and ear tufts erect and menacing. I’d seen other owl species adopt this posture in order to look bigger and more threatening to an aggressor, and it was working: this was a creature braced for battle. I was taken aback, as I still am every time I see one of these birds, by how enormous it was. The beast stood immobile, glaring at us with yellow eyes in the winter dark and illuminated unevenly by Sergey’s light as our pace quickened. Everything was silent except for the rhythmic friction of skis on snow and our gasps of exhaustion. The urgency to reach the owl before it freed itself was palpable.</p><p>My heart stopped as the fish owl pivoted and took to the air in retreat, but the weight of the noose carpet held and drew the bird softly back to ground. The huge owl moved away from us with awkward bounds along the broad, snowy bank, dragging the noose carpet with it, until finally, when we were only meters away, the raptor spun onto its back on the river’s edge. It lay there facing us, talons extended and agape, ready to shred any flesh within striking distance.</p><p>In the off-season, I’d trained in raptor handling at The Raptor Center at the University of Minnesota and had learned that hesitation with a defensive raptor doesn’t do anyone any good. I swooped my arm in a fluid motion the moment I was within reach, scooping the bird up by its extended legs. Upside down and confused, the owl relaxed its wings, and I used my free arm to tuck them first against its body and then the body against me as though holding a swaddled newborn child. The owl was ours.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 28 Aug 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LRg-pKPKQcvaQWYUPeCkjgv5LsQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/YB5ZRCR4KBG45HNF6Z6LSDYE4A.jpg","linkMd5":"f0b7bf16449148d5ee10ee63c530667d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn80@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-06-35-097_2ca835b976c1a523.webp","destWidth":2000,"destHeight":1333,"sourceBytes":339187,"destBytes":311422,"author":"Jonathan Slaght","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LRg-pKPKQcvaQWYUPeCkjgv5LsQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/YB5ZRCR4KBG45HNF6Z6LSDYE4A.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn80@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-06-35-097_2ca835b976c1a523.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/8RckKFtk67jcakcXUe5rZyavv3k=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/UM7Z37RRIBA3PC5LE2EUHLYVM4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn32@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-07-01-991_78fdfb7ad5a8f0f7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394622},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Make every project a breeze with the right miter saw ","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/shop/miter-saw/","description":"<img alt=\"Great for woodworking.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"625\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/-_SPOd5Q5BuaEeOauaEAP2m_zuY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/XVVWL57PZJHZDDEY3QJF3PPXG4.jpg\" width=\"938\"/><br/><caption>Great for woodworking. (Vance Osterhout via Unsplash/)</caption><p>These days, you don’t need to be a full woodworker to get the right tool for your home project. Thankfully, many brands have simplified the process and it’s easy to pick the right miter saw for your next build. In case you’re wondering what a miter saw does, it’s a specialized power tool that helps you cut wood at different angles—think for crown molding, garden beds, door frames, and more. The key to those angled cuts is, of course, the blade mounted on the swing arm of the miter saw that moves left and right. Miter saws come equipped with all the gadgets to help you make the most precise cut, including lasers, measuring setting, and more. Keep on reading to see what’s good on the market right now.</p><img alt=\"Everything you need.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/0ro_IFXLMCJzG6HK_iPGwnSpJZw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/G35MHGA46FGIRNRDZMYTIZLHGM.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>Everything you need. (Amazon/)</caption><p>If you’re looking for a basic miter saw at an affordable price, this <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/DEWALT-DWS779-Sliding-Compound-Miter/dp/B01ESCU5WS/ref=as_li_ss_tl?ie=UTF8&#38;linkCode=ll1&#38;tag=popularscience-20&#38;linkId=0befa6c0d4121d2d6900314ebcaf6709&#38;language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\">DeWALT Sliding Compound Miter Saw</a> will help you get those cuts just right. The back fence helps you cut up to 2x14 wood at a 90-degree angle and 2x12 at a 45-degree angle, without a laser guide. The best part? Its dust collection system captures up to 75 percent of dust generated by your project so clean up is easy. No confusing features to get stuck on, just a straightforward power tool that helps you get the job done.</p><img alt=\"Your wallet will be happy.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/9r5lh2mDn3ExOBQBu5R2yYv-MS4=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/D3CCAKQ3HFGZ7MMT3NKP25PQUI.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>Your wallet will be happy. (Amazon/)</caption><p>Hitachi has new branding now—Metabo HPT—and <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Metabo-HPT-C10FCGS-Compound-15-Amp/dp/B07PX44JQM/ref=as_li_ss_tl?ie=UTF8&#38;linkCode=ll1&#38;tag=popularscience-20&#38;linkId=b3902502b7222a71c6a846c0306f0655&#38;language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\">this miter saw</a> puts the power in powerful. At a great price, this 10-inch compound miter saw allows you to get the most precise cuts thanks to its thumb activated stop for adjustments. You get a bevel range of up to 45 degrees and the horizontal handle and clamping system helps you get a good grip on your lumber as you cut down. It’s a great power tool for your beginner workshop as you go through your list of home projects.</p><img alt=\"Cordless cuts.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/jA1veyiEFCqnd2mhaFUOs_EJHkY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/PLUEZEUUJJEVDLIP3UPEG4VVUU.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>Cordless cuts. (Amazon/)</caption><p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B07LFPYPCQ/ref=as_li_ss_tl?ie=UTF8&#38;linkCode=ll1&#38;tag=popularscience-20&#38;linkId=52d4140d8aa8e3b5e5d0de7442f02603&#38;language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\">A cordless miter saw</a> gives you the freedom to set up anywhere you want to work on the project you need to complete. Despite being battery-operated, you don’t lose any of that cutting power in the process. You can easily do an eight-inch cross cut at 90 degrees and cut lumber, hardwoods, trim, and more without much effort. It’s lightweight at just 22 pounds and the side handles make it easy to carry. The attached dust bag keeps everything clean as well and your preferred adjustments stay put thanks to the lockable knobs.</p><img alt=\"For the professionals.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/Rz9GBROYzL1SGmLKJ9zhQlrw6Kg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/NYAJFIHKL5DUFDR2RZ46CVMBYQ.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>For the professionals. (Amazon/)</caption><p>For the pros that like to take their work home, high standards come with the territory. <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Bosch-120-Volt-12-Inch-Dual-Bevel-GCM12SD/dp/B004323NNC/ref=as_li_ss_tl?ie=UTF8&#38;linkCode=ll1&#38;tag=popularscience-20&#38;linkId=3f5c81896d2f7bb527246e7eb836eee7&#38;language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\">This dual-bevel sliding miter saw</a> comes with a glide system that allows for wider, more precise crosscuts. At about 65 pounds, it’s still approachable enough to take site to site with the help of a work table (sold separately).</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Aug 2020 19:14:53 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/-_SPOd5Q5BuaEeOauaEAP2m_zuY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/XVVWL57PZJHZDDEY3QJF3PPXG4.jpg","linkMd5":"bd9aeac122f021dec378b9f79409b89b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn100@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-06-54-732_07dd58bf134c264a.webp","destWidth":938,"destHeight":625,"sourceBytes":98669,"destBytes":79406,"author":"PopSci Commerce Team","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/-_SPOd5Q5BuaEeOauaEAP2m_zuY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/XVVWL57PZJHZDDEY3QJF3PPXG4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn100@2020_1/2020/08/31/05-06-54-732_07dd58bf134c264a.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/0ro_IFXLMCJzG6HK_iPGwnSpJZw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/G35MHGA46FGIRNRDZMYTIZLHGM.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn8@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-07-04-324_bf42ee52327906a2.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/9r5lh2mDn3ExOBQBu5R2yYv-MS4=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/D3CCAKQ3HFGZ7MMT3NKP25PQUI.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn19@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-03-198_a72ff44dd306f89c.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/jA1veyiEFCqnd2mhaFUOs_EJHkY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/PLUEZEUUJJEVDLIP3UPEG4VVUU.jpg":null,"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/Rz9GBROYzL1SGmLKJ9zhQlrw6Kg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/NYAJFIHKL5DUFDR2RZ46CVMBYQ.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn68@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-07-01-964_567214417ba11c21.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394628},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"What various weird computer noises mean for your machine","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/noises-computer-repair/","description":"<img alt='\"Help! I need somebody... Help!\"' data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/rcudgvp84LHSg-2CfgmDtYZ7YLc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/I572FOEWQZBMTOGJTD36JR6BAQ.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>\"Help! I need somebody... Help!\" ( benjamin lehman / Unsplash/)</caption><p>No PC is truly silent, but your computer shouldn’t be noisier than your lawnmower. If you have to turn up your music just to drown out the whirring or grinding noises your computer makes, you may want to look into that—it could be the first signal of some serious problems.</p><p>Knowing how to tell different hard drive noises apart and learning what they might mean, can save your computer. Or at least give you an unequivocal pass to buy a new one.</p><p>Clicking or grinding noises</p><p>Let’s start with the most worrisome sound. If your computer starts to click, grind, or make any sort of low-pitched buzzing noise, you should stop what you’re doing and check the hard drive. This sound could indicate a dying disk. Do not ignore this sound.</p><p>To check your drive’s health, I recommend a third-party tool like <a href=\"https://crystalmark.info/en/software/crystaldiskinfo/\" target=\"_blank\">CrystalDiskInfo</a> (Windows) or <a href=\"https://binaryfruit.com/drivedx\" target=\"_blank\">DriveDx</a> (macOS). Fire up the program, click on each of your drives in the menu, and make sure they’re all listed as “Good.” If it indicates your drive is anything less than that, you should back up all your data as soon as possible. You may still have some time to do so—occasionally a drive marked “Caution” can still run for years, but if it’s making noises, the drive’s death might be close. Once all your files are safe, consider replacing your drive with an <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Crucial-MX500-500GB-NAND-Internal/dp/B0786QNS9B\" target=\"_blank\">SSD</a>—not only will it likely last longer, but it’ll make your computer feel much faster. If your hard drives are healthy, take the incident as a warning and avoid any unpleasant surprises by <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/back-up-and-protect-your-data/\" target=\"_blank\">backing up your data regularly</a>, because as it happens to any living creature, all hard drives will die one day.</p><p>One you’ve ruled out your hard drive as the culprit, you’ll need to dig a little deeper to find the source of that clicking sound. If your computer still has a DVD drive, then it could be in the process of failing, and needs repair <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/repair-or-replace-computer/\" target=\"_blank\">or replacement</a>.</p><p>Finally, in a lot of desktop PCs, a clicking noise could just mean a cable has gotten too close to a fan, and is getting hit repeatedly by the blades. If you’re comfortable with a screwdriver, open up your PC and make sure the fans are clear of obstructions.</p><p>Loud whirring noises</p><p>If there’s one sound every computer user knows well, it’s the loud whoosh that comes from an overzealous fan running at full speed. On a desktop PC, this will likely be low-to-medium pitch like the video above, and on a laptop, it could be higher-pitched and much more annoying. The smaller the fan, the louder and higher pitched that noise will be.</p><p>If you’re hearing this, there’s a very good chance your machine just needs some cooling adjustment. You might also want to check your hard drive’s health, just in case, since <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oGygwiprEbA\" target=\"_blank\">a drive spinning up and down repeatedly</a> could create a similar sound. Finally, check to see if there’s a disc spinning in the DVD drive—sometimes those can be obnoxiously loud even if they’re working properly.</p><p>If your fans are indeed too loud, start by <b>opening Task Manager in Windows (Ctrl+Shift+Esc) or Activity Monitor in macOS (under Applications &#62; Utilities)</b> and see if any applications are taking up a large portion of your CPU. If your computer’s working hard, the fans will work hard to cool it, so you may have a program running in the background you forgot about, or malware eating up resources without you knowing. Close the offending program or <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/remove-malware-from-computer/\" target=\"_blank\">run a malware scan</a> to see if that helps.</p><p>If your computer is idle and still making fan noise, it could be overheating. A program like <a href=\"https://www.alcpu.com/CoreTemp/\" target=\"_blank\">Core Temp</a> (Windows) or <a href=\"https://www.fannywidget.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Fanny</a> (macOS) can tell you if your CPU is running hot. As a ballpark, if you aren’t running anything strenuous and your CPU is 70 degrees celsius or higher, I’d say that’s abnormal, and will likely cause excessive fan noise. If you’re using your laptop in bed, make sure to put a tray or anything solid under it—your clothes, skin or blankets might be preventing the system from cooling off, making the fans work harder and louder. The same happens with accumulated dust, so give the fan grilles a few passes with a <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Strong-Cleaning-Screens-Photographic-Equipment/dp/B01D9OXY6K\" target=\"_blank\">dust blower</a>, or better yet, an <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/XPOWER-Airrow-Multi-Use-Electric-Computer/dp/B01BI4UQK0\" target=\"_blank\">electric duster</a>. If you feel comfortable, you might even want to open up your machine and wipe the dust off the fan with a clean microfiber cloth.</p><p>Finally, if you have a desktop PC—especially one you built yourself—you may just need to adjust the fan curves in the BIOS. Some of your fans may be running at 75% or 100% all the time by default, which is unnecessary. Or, the curves may be set in a weird spot that causes the fans to constantly ramp up for a few seconds to cool the CPU, but then they ramp back down, allowing the CPU to get hot again. Press *Delete* as your computer starts to enter the BIOS screen, and look for any fan control settings you can play with—try a lower setting, but don’t set them too low, lest your temperatures get too high.</p><p>Sounds coming from your speakers, even when you aren’t playing anything</p><p>Put your ear closer to the sound—is it coming from inside your computer or is it coming from the speakers? Speakers are supposed to make sound, but if you’re hearing noise from them even when your computer isn’t playing audio, something might be wrong. Make sure the speaker cable is plugged all the way in to your PC—you’ll usually hear a click that tells you the cable is fully connected, but sometimes you have to really shove it to get that last millimeter in. If that doesn’t work, you can troubleshoot your speakers by plugging them into another device (like your phone) to see if the sound persists. The problem could be in your speaker’s cable, or it could just be feedback from the internals of your PC, in which case a <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/FiiO-E10K-Headphone-Amplifier-Black/dp/B00LP3AMC2\" target=\"_blank\">USB sound card, also known as a DAC</a>, might help.</p><p>You might also be experiencing a <a href=\"https://www.techhive.com/article/3063590/how-to-get-rid-of-hum-and-eliminate-other-noises-from-your-audio-and-video-systems.html\" target=\"_blank\">ground loop</a>, in which case a <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/RadioShack-System-Isolator-Electric-reducer/dp/B002YGM5D8\" target=\"_blank\">ground loop isolator</a> can reduce the noise. I’ve even had speakers that <a href=\"https://www.seattletimes.com/business/computer-speakers-capturing-radio-signal/\" target=\"_blank\">picked up faint radio signals</a> due to poorly shielded cables, which is a remarkably spooky experience.</p><p>Ultimately, there are so many things that can cause unwanted noise from speakers that we could probably write a whole article on the subject—but if you play around with your connections, you may be able to narrow down the source, and replace the offending component.</p><p>Buzzing or screeching noises</p><p>If none of the above have fixed the problem, but you’re still hearing a buzzing sound, it could be just about anything. Heck, if they’re mild enough, any of the above problems could be described as “buzzing,” so check your hard drive and running processes before you move on.</p><p>Once you’ve discarded all the scenarios above, my first guess would be that you’re experiencing coil whine—one of my least favorite noises in computing, since there’s often little you can do about it.</p><p>Coil whine happens when the coils in your components start vibrating at just the right frequency to start making a very annoying noise. It can be anywhere from <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8kjhQrOtdF8\" target=\"_blank\">a low-pitched buzz</a> to <a href=\"https://youtu.be/HP73edpQwgc?t=105\" target=\"_blank\">a high pitched squeal</a>, and often happens when your computer is under load. If you can pinpoint the source, you might be able to mitigate the noise, at least to some degree. For example, in gaming PCs, coil whine commonly comes from the graphics card when it’s doing a lot of work, in which case you can decrease your graphics settings or turn on VSync to lighten the load. I’ve also heard coil whine on a lot of power adapters—if you move your laptop or monitor’s power brick further away, it may not be as annoying. Some laptop manufacturers may even offer other power adapters without the old school “brick” that may coil whine less. In other cases, there’s nothing you can do, and you’ll have to suffer through the noise.</p><p>If you aren’t convinced the buzzing sound is coil whine, you might also look back at your fans. If they are vibrating against the steel case of your desktop PC, some <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Cosmos-Pieces-Rubber-Mounting-Screws/dp/B00H9905KA\" target=\"_blank\">rubber dampeners</a> may help stop the sound. Your fans may also need lubrication, so a small drop of <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B00065VGWK\" target=\"_blank\">sewing machine oil</a> in <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hPyHBH3WwBM\" target=\"_blank\">the bearing of an old fan</a> can make it run like new again. And if you have one of the ever-so-trendy <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Corsair-Radiator-Software-Control-Liquid/dp/B0829RTMKY\" target=\"_blank\">all-in-one liquid cooling units</a> in your gaming PC, make sure it’s <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BbGomv195sk\" target=\"_blank\">mounted properly</a>, and check the manual to see if the pump is adjustable—turning the pump speed down could lessen the buzzing sound it makes.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Sun, 30 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/rcudgvp84LHSg-2CfgmDtYZ7YLc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/I572FOEWQZBMTOGJTD36JR6BAQ.jpg","linkMd5":"95070af2e4b5dadda80a14dc7996fb69","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn39@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-06-35-071_a8fc5edad34625d5.webp","destWidth":2000,"destHeight":1500,"sourceBytes":100037,"destBytes":66352,"author":"Whitson Gordon","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/rcudgvp84LHSg-2CfgmDtYZ7YLc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/I572FOEWQZBMTOGJTD36JR6BAQ.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn39@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-06-35-071_a8fc5edad34625d5.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394631},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Watch Elon Musk unveil the next version of his AI-powered brain implant, Neuralink","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/technology/elon-musk-neuralink-brain-ai-chip-announcement/","description":"<p>We first heard about Neuralink back in 2017. Non-verbal people could operate communication devices without the need for hands. We could jam our Spotify playlists directly into our grey matter. We’re human hardware. </p><p>Musk showed off a version of it at a demonstration last year, but this week, he has been crowing about how V2 will “blow minds.” He claims that the demo will include a working device that will demonstrate the capabilities of the system in its current state. But, the developments haven’t just come from sci-fi. Startups like Kernel and massive tech behemoths such as Facebook are all currently working on the tech in some form or another.</p><p>So far, the technology has shown promise, but it takes a lot to convince someone to let the Geek Squad dig into your skull and start attaching electrodes where there was once only squishy flesh. Musk claims the procedure will be similar to getting Lasik vision surgery, which actually doesn’t sound like too big of an ask, especially if it can delivery on some of its loftier promises, like treating serious brain diseases. Down the road, however, he hopes it will become common place enough that it can become an upgrade process instead of a medical treatment. We’ll excitedly download our new brain firmware to find out what new capacity we have to play with. </p><p>The event</p><p>We’re finally getting started at 6:41 p.m. Eastern. He says it will cause zero noticeable neural damage when you insert the link. It allegedly won’t bleed. </p><p>He just brought out three pigs. That’s far down the road.
