Horaczek","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/iekMwhlugHcqJOlnAyEKl6JHbjw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/U6ZPZSRAAFHEDKOQNDV7O5PUU4.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn13@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-06-34-907_f9313492c0d6aa89.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/hcjOX1L9HsgHPc1nX3EKcSiHB8Y=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/SK6US2CKUJCX3KD6U7HWPREBSQ.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn80@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-07-01-961_5fff40275d937750.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/28uMz_FKEFX4H0RgmRMN_27vn_w=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/QUSDP22XJRAKFGM5DK2TUSNMTQ.png":null,"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/9ct-H4_Xj55I6RwuOFOqCYvH08A=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/2G6TWZGU5VBXJDG5MXQJOEL3OU.png":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394631},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Mount Rainier’s first wolverine mama in a century is a sign of the species’ comeback","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/science/wolverines-rainier-return/","description":"<img alt=\"A mother wolverine and her kits ascend a tree.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1303\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/6rVOt8oY5qUbu3N4pA0t8mF0H7Y=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/JPUCDWOGYNAVXHLCCCBQGKBNJU.jpg\" width=\"1737\"/><br/><caption>A mother wolverine and her kits ascend a tree. (NPS/Cascades Carnivore Project/)</caption><p>Wolverines hadn’t been spotted in the forests of Mount Rainier National Park in over 100 years. These animals are so rare, wildlife ecologists struggle even now to determine how vulnerable or endangered they are. So when park wildlife ecologist Tara Chestnut and her colleagues received numerous credible reports of wolverine sightings from hikers, they set out to install a camera in hopes of catching video proof.</p><p>The first camera went up in the fall of 2018. It took a year and a half and five additional camera installations, but the payoff was worth it. When they checked their footage this past spring, the park staff found not only video of a wolverine, but a lactating wolverine (who they have named Joni) with two baby kits. “This is the first reproductive female that we’ve ever documented in the park, and the fact that she was breeding was just a really pleasant surprise,” says Chestnut. “We were just thrilled.”</p><p>With their cameras, Chestnut and her team also spotted a male, who they’ve nicknamed “Van.” But the fact that they found a reproductive wolverine with not one, but two kits, is the biggest happy surprise. Wolverine habitats have become so segmented over the years by cities and highways, she says, but these four wolverines are a sign that different interventions—like the wildlife bridges across the I-90 highway—are an indication that Mount Rainier National Park and its neighbors’ efforts to usher the animals back are not in vain. </p><p>“The last historic report of a wolverine in Mount Rainier National Park is from 1915, it was a trapping record from a park naturalist at the time,” Chestnut says. The soaring prices for wolverine pelts made them popular targets for hunters, she explains, so the wolverine population plummeted throughout the 20th century. Wolverines were hunted so extensively that now there are now only 300 to 1,000 of them in the lower 48 US states, according to Mount Rainier National Park’s <a href=\"https://www.nps.gov/mora/learn/news/wolverines-return-to-mount-rainier-national-park-after-more-than-100-years.htm\">press release</a>.</p><p>Chestnut says that wolverines are sensitive, shy animals that need a lot of help to recolonize the wilderness they were hunted out of. Seeing the mother and her kits on film is encouraging, but it will be a long journey to usher more animals back to the park. A big part of that effort is documenting where and when wolverines pass through. </p><html><body><blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\"><p dir=\"ltr\" lang=\"en\">Wolverines Return to Mount Rainier National Park After More Than 100 Years, News Release: <a href=\"https://t.co/qmCkTDsFAU\">https://t.co/qmCkTDsFAU</a><br/><br/>Video of three wolverines at the end of a snowfield then running through a meadow into a forest. Credit: Travis Harris -kl <a href=\"https://t.co/ALwJoAOmTG\">pic.twitter.com/ALwJoAOmTG</a></p>— MountRainierNPS (@MountRainierNPS) <a href=\"https://twitter.com/MountRainierNPS/status/1296507311800942593?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">August 20, 2020</a></blockquote>\n<script async=\"\" charset=\"utf-8\" src=\"https://platform.twitter.com/widgets.js\"></script>\n</body></html><p>“If someone visiting the park is lucky enough to see a wolverine and get a picture, we definitely want that reported,” Chestnut says. “We have an <a href=\"https://arcg.is/rLOiy\">online database</a> where people can enter those reports.” Citizen science is a big part of park ecology science, she says, so if visitors see and photograph tracks or scat, or participate in surveys like with the <a href=\"http://www.cascadeswolverineproject.org/\">Cascades Wolverine Project</a>, reports that ecologists can follow up on.</p><p>“A wolverine’s poop is their calling card,” says Chestnut. The animals can use their scat not only as a way to communicate with each other about their territories and reproductive status, she says, but scientists can analyze the scat and use it to identify distinct individuals. “That way we can get good estimates of the population.”</p><p>The more data collected, the more we’ll be able to see whether this year’s sightings are part of a long-term recovery or if it’s just a fluke, says Chestnut. Scientists cannot understand wolverine migratory patterns if they don’t see when they move and where they’re going, she says, but since the animals are so elusive, scientists won’t get that information without these super vigilant tracking efforts.</p><p>But these searches, like almost everything, have been waylaid by the pandemic. Female wolverines use snowfall in the winter to build their natal dens, where they birth and take care of their young. The team at Mount Rainier was supposed to look for these snow caves at the end of the spring, after the animals have left but before the snow all melts. Chestnut says that finding and measuring these dens would have provided insight as to how the mothers choose their nesting locations, and helped them protect areas of their park that could serve as dens in the future. Alas, park staff will have to wait until next year to conduct their search.</p><p>Because of their reliance on snowfall for their natal dens, wolverines are a very climate-vulnerable species, says Chestnut, and the warming climate is shrinking their viable habitats. It’s important to preserve the full suite of wildlife, she says, and we’re trying, but it’s too early to celebrate their full comeback.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 28 Aug 2020 12:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/6rVOt8oY5qUbu3N4pA0t8mF0H7Y=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/JPUCDWOGYNAVXHLCCCBQGKBNJU.jpg","linkMd5":"21db6c075bba8ceec9bdb5c98897f17b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn78@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-06-35-558_ea2b9dd96c874e17.webp","destWidth":1737,"destHeight":1303,"sourceBytes":541418,"destBytes":447276,"author":"Hannah Seo","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/6rVOt8oY5qUbu3N4pA0t8mF0H7Y=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/JPUCDWOGYNAVXHLCCCBQGKBNJU.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn78@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-06-35-558_ea2b9dd96c874e17.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394633},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"California and the Forest Service have a plan to prevent future catastrophic fires","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/environment/california-wildfire-management-agreement/","description":"<img alt=\"A road during the Jones Fire\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"3746\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LJWYIB_dLZdQtGdX2WagN-ojpKg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/7UKSVDQQTJE5PP5UPVPGDA2YLY.jpg\" width=\"4848\"/><br/><caption>A road during the Jones Fire (Marcus Kauffman/)</caption><p>As hundreds of fires scorch California, state officials and federal forest agents <a href=\"https://www.gov.ca.gov/wp-content/uploads/2020/08/8.12.20-CA-Shared-Stewardship-MOU.pdf\">signed an agreement</a> that may help the state be able to better weather future fire seasons.</p><p>Right now, <a href=\"https://www.latimes.com/california/story/2020-08-23/massive-california-firestorms-expected-to-worsen-with-more-lightning-winds\">the second and third largest fires</a> in the state’s history are ripping through coastal forests and inland shrubland in Northern California. The flames have claimed more than 1,100 homes and other buildings, along with <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/08/25/us/california-fires.html\">1.4 million acres of land</a>. </p><p>Climate change is culpable here. While fire is a normal and necessary part of much of California’s forest and rangelands, the region’s trend toward <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/california-wet-dry-season/\">heightened wet and dry extremes</a>, coupled with overall warmer conditions, make the state especially primed for high-intensity burns. </p><p>But the way forests have been managed for the past 100 years hasn’t helped the situation. Prior to the Gold Rush, fires were a part of the landscape—ignited by both lightning strikes and Native Americans. Around four million acres burned every year on average, and smoky skies in summer and fall were <a href=\"https://nature.berkeley.edu/stephenslab/wp-content/uploads/2015/04/Stephens-et-al.-CA-fire-area-FEM-2007.pdf\">probably not unusual</a>. After settlers forcibly removed Indigenous people from the land, their cultural burning practices were eventually <a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2020/08/24/899422710/to-manage-wildfire-california-looks-to-what-tribes-have-known-all-along\">replaced by policies of full-on fire suppression</a>. Between the 1930s and 1970s, the U.S. Forest Service and state officials maintained a mindset of dousing all blazes.</p><p>But periodic, generally lower-intensity fires are important for distributing nutrients, helping seeds germinate, and managing disease. They also prevent the build-up of small trees, shrubs and woody material, which otherwise cause the fires that do spark to become especially large and intense. In response, management has been changing in recent decades, albeit at a slow pace. The <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/science/not-enough-prescribed-burns-californiia/\">perceived risks about the process and high costs</a> have blocked an aggressive statewide policy to reintroduce fire and reduce fuels.</p><p>Now, as smoke chokes much of the northern and central part of the state, officials in Sacramento have signed an agreement that appears to do just that. In the agreement, Forest Service agents, the California Natural Resources Agency Secretary, and Governor Gavin Newsom committed to “shared stewardship” of the state’s forests and rangelands. Most strikingly, the non-binding memo names a goal of thinning, burning, or otherwise treating vegetation across one million acres annually by 2025 (with federal and state agencies each contributing 500,000 acres).  For comparison, prescribed burns in California totalled <a href=\"https://www.nifc.gov/fireInfo/fireInfo_statistics.html\">less than 50,000 acres in 2017</a> (though that stat doesn’t include those wildfires that were allowed to burn or tree thinning).</p><p>“This kind of commitment is a really great step,” says Rebecca Miller, an environmental scientist at Stanford University studying wildfire policies. “Restoring healthy forests and rangelands is a great move forward.” In <a href=\"https://resources.ca.gov/CNRALegacyFiles/wp-content/uploads/2018/05/California-Forest-Carbon-Plan-Final-Draft-for-Public-Release-May-2018.pdf\">a 2018 forest carbon plan</a>, state officials included a goal to treat 60,000 acres a year on non-federal lands, and noted that 500,000 acres was an “aspirational goal.” So the new agreement seems to be an effort to turn that aspiration into action, and commit the Forest Service to matching the effort. Across California, <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/science/not-enough-prescribed-burns-californiia/\">approximately 20 million acres</a> are thought to be in need of some kind of treatment, such as managed fires or thinning.</p><p>The agreement also addressed a key barrier to broad-scale action: coordination between different land managers. California forests and shrublands encompass a patchwork of private, state, and federal management. Sometimes that quilt of management strategies is visible from the sky. “Sometimes, if you have an aerial image, there’s a line you can see when it goes from Forest Service to private land, or from Forest Service to national park, just because whatever has been done on that land is so different,” says Emily Moran, a plant ecologist at the University of California, Merced. She says that at one research site of hers, the trees are visibly a different color between private and federal groves when viewed from above. “Fire and other factors don’t respect those boundaries,” adds Moran. “I think it’s encouraging to try and get people to talk to each other more.”</p><p>The agreement emphasizes the need for agencies to collaborate on restoration, since fire is indifferent to jurisdictional boundaries. This could include setting shared goals, consolidating data, and working together to monitor ecosystems and support long-term research projects. For private landowners, the agencies proposed streamlining permitting processes and providing technical assistance to make it less expensive and easier to thin stands. This could be crucial: Miller points out that 39 percent of forests in the state are privately-owned. If an agency, for example, wanted to reduce woody fuels around the perimeter of a town vulnerable to fires, one uncooperative landowner can spoil the effort. “If you get a little bit of resistance, it can complicate efforts to conduct your burn,” says Miller. </p><p>Of course, this all comes with a price tag. The agreement notes a few ways to recoup funds. One is to “improve” timber harvest—the statement is vaguely worded but appears to suggest increased logging. “California has some of the highest environmental standards for timber harvest in the world, producing California lumber could decrease demand for timber harvested with lower ecological standards,” the agreement reads. “Given California’s increasing housing needs and greenhouse gas emission goals, California has a direct interest in consuming ecologically sourced lumber.”</p><p>That provision may give environmental groups reason to worry. “The question is how you do [tree thinning] in a way that’s responsible and driven by science and not driven by the political demands of the logging industry,” Kathryn Phillips, executive director of Sierra Club California, <a href=\"https://www.mercurynews.com/2020/08/23/california-fires-state-feds-agree-to-thin-millions-of-acres-of-forests/\">told the <i>The Mercury News</i></a>. </p><p>A more creative funding idea floated in the agreement is to somehow monetize the small trees, twigs, and other bits scraped up into piles during thinning treatments. Right now, those piles present a fire risk and are costly to manage. But there might be other options, such as a market for chopping debris into plywood material or burning them for biofuel. “That could provide funding that’s needed in order to treat all the areas,” says Moran. “But you would also have to be very careful you don’t create any sort of perverse incentives to overharvest.”</p><p>The agreement also provides for continued research throughout these efforts. Moran says that it will be important to continue analyzing how different treatments affect factors like fire behavior and biodiversity. Generally, managed or prescribed flames tend to mimic natural conditions better than thinning treatments, but sometimes fire might be impractical or unpopular (for example, due to concerns about air quality). </p><p>Even with the warmer and drier conditions we’re baking into the region, Moran says that management can make a big difference for ecology as well as safety. “If fuel is more spread out and patchy, it is still going to be harder for fires to get up to the really huge sizes we’ve been seeing some recent cases,” she says. “So my instinct is—and what the research seems to be suggesting at this point—is that management is still going to be helpful, but we are still going to be seeing effects [of climate change].”</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 27 Aug 2020 10:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LJWYIB_dLZdQtGdX2WagN-ojpKg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/7UKSVDQQTJE5PP5UPVPGDA2YLY.jpg","linkMd5":"3515e33644221a5c0af0ef0731ff9f1b","destWidth":4848,"destHeight":3746,"sourceBytes":2291560,"destBytes":2305492,"author":"Ula Chrobak","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/LJWYIB_dLZdQtGdX2WagN-ojpKg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/7UKSVDQQTJE5PP5UPVPGDA2YLY.jpg":null},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394627},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"10 classic motorcycles to drool over","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/cars/classic-honda-motorcycles-photos/","description":"<img alt=\"Honda CBX engine.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/3s8Ljf5FNfwVRkbBFkD-izi6J0c=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/C7ZXMYOFIVFY5IJOX2MW6EI4M4.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>Honda CBX engine. (Honda/)</caption><p><i>This story originally featured on </i><a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/story/news/top-10-classic-honda-motorcycles/\" target=\"_blank\">Motorcyclist</a>.</p><p>For a <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/new-motorcycles/\" target=\"_blank\">motorcycle</a> to stand the test of time it needs to be in some way significant to the motorcycle world. Some motorcycles prove their import by the length of their production run. Others, particularly ahead-of-their-time motorcycles, prove their worth by increasing their appeal long after their production run ends. It seems like <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/new-honda-motorcycles/\" target=\"_blank\">Honda</a> has produced an inordinate number of motorcycles of lasting significance and interest.</p><p>The following list is by no means comprehensive of the all-time great Honda motorcycles—and, in fact, excludes some of the most iconic, collectible motorcycles that Big Red has ever built. Rare or homologation-spec bikes like the NR750, <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/smart-money-1990-honda-rc30/\" target=\"_blank\">RC30</a> or RC45, VFR400, NSR250R, etc., are not present here. Instead, this list comprises models that for the most part are readily available on the used market.</p><p>Some of the bikes included have persisted in Honda’s lineup for decades; others had short production runs that only served to increase their current desirability. The hard part: narrowing the list down to just 10.</p><p>Honda Super Cub</p><img alt=\"The 2020 Honda Super Cub C125 ABS. The latest version of the most used motor vehicle in the world.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/CLuUrqSJhusTL1JMxIssgAxiRZw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/DITU7VTSF5BNNGAKLKNKHQV72Q.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>The 2020 Honda Super Cub C125 ABS. The latest version of the most used motor vehicle in the world. (Honda/)</caption><p>If there’s one motorcycle that has been immune to the march of time, it’s the Honda Super Cub. In production (in various generations) since 1958, Honda has sold more than 100 million units of the little step-through. Which reportedly makes it the highest-selling motor vehicle in history.</p><p>When Honda reintroduced the <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/2019-honda-super-cub-c125-abs-first-ride-review/\" target=\"_blank\">Super Cub C125 ABS</a> to the US market in 2019, it seemed like things came full circle. For decades, the Super Cub had been a workhouse in developing nations, where its utility and affordability made it ubiquitous—far removed from the spirit of leisure that it inspired in American riders in the 1960s. These days, its practicality is just as appealing to American riders looking for basic, easy transportation as is its reputation for “niceness.” You may meet the nicest people on a Honda, but you’ll also meet practical people on a Honda. Not that the two are mutually exclusive.</p><p>2004 Honda RC51</p><img alt=\"Pictured: 2002 Honda RC51. From the era of great V-twin racebikes.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/6U_TkJbv1Vw_JgKGJ-e9qmrYWu8=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/TXZ2CC5PAZFGRODE5ZLWYVLAOE.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>Pictured: 2002 Honda RC51. From the era of great V-twin racebikes. (Honda/)</caption><p>Back in the 1990s, 1,000cc twins versus 750cc fours seemed a fair fight on paper, but Japanese fours, including Honda’s built-to-win RC45 homologation special, were getting clobbered by a certain plucky Italian V-twin. The Japanese industrial machine outdone by a bunch of chain-smoking, long lunch-taking, summer holiday-going Italians? This was not to be borne. So to take advantage of a rule book that favored the Italian twin, Honda threw its hands in the air, went to the drawing board, and came up with <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/2004-honda-rc51-review/\" target=\"_blank\">the RC51</a>.</p><p>When it hit showroom floors at the dawn of the new millennium with a price tag of $9,999, it put the RC moniker within reach of the (relative) masses and looked like a bargain next to the Italian competition—which it promptly started beating on racetracks around the world. For race fans, the RC51 will always be associated with its two most notable protagonists: Colin Edwards and Nicky Hayden. These days, it’s like riding racing history. The most coveted model is the SP2, particularly the 2004 Nicky Hayden replica variant.</p><p>2001 Honda Gold Wing</p><img alt=\"The 2001 Honda Gold Wing is still an amazingly competent motorcycle. In the right hands, it can put to shame many sportier bikes in the twisties.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/gGrpLtxBmM1S_LQDMZD7MatoQzY=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/4ER7ZKBS3NC7FCE4BSX53CCSZU.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>The 2001 Honda Gold Wing is still an amazingly competent motorcycle. In the right hands, it can put to shame many sportier bikes in the twisties. (Honda/)</caption><p>Depending on who you’re talking to, the Gold Wing could be considered Honda’s flagship motorcycle. For a company whose heritage is firmly entrenched in racing, that’s a testament to the popularity of the Wing. Now in its sixth generation, the Gold Wing hardly resembles the original four-cylinder, fairing-less machine of 1974.</p><p>The Gold Wing practically created the heavyweight touring category and is still the benchmark for two-up, long-distance touring. Honda built a gajillion of the previous <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/2001-2004-honda-gold-wing/\" target=\"_blank\">generation GL1800 Gold Wings</a> (2001–2017), so if you’re looking for impressive touring performance for a song, chances are you can find one for the right price.</p><p>1994 Honda VFR750F (RC36-2)</p><img alt=\"While the fuel-injected VFR800F is also highly lauded, the final carbureted Veefer is (to those of us who rode them) the pinnacle of VFR-dom.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/nm7q44EseoeHA5qlbqHB3J-miII=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/DZH5EKMT7VEPZEAX3RNIUI627M.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>While the fuel-injected VFR800F is also highly lauded, the final carbureted Veefer is (to those of us who rode them) the pinnacle of VFR-dom. (Honda/)</caption><p>When it comes to Honda V-4 sportbikes, motorcyclists have long wondered “what if?”</p><p>The original Interceptor eventually gave way to the RC30 (VFR750R) homologation special and the more street-oriented VFR750F. Over time, the division has become even more pronounced (see: the MotoGP-derived $184,000 RC213V-S and the staid VFR1200X “adventure” bike).</p><p>By splicing the Interceptor family tree into two branches—the unattainable and the “plebeian”—and by focusing its sporting endeavors on its inline-four-powered CBRs, Honda denied consumers one of their greatest dreams: a mass-produced V-4 race-replica sportbike.</p><p>But I’m getting ahead of myself. Back to the VFR750F. In the ’90s, the VFR was one heck of a motorcycle: “plebeian” only in its price and availability. From ’90–‘97, the VFR showed up on<i> </i><a href=\"https://www.cycleworld.com/ten-best-motorcycles/\" target=\"_blank\"><i>Cycle World</i>’s Ten Best</a> list every single year. It’s not too much of a stretch to claim it’s one of the most universally acclaimed Hondas of all time thanks to its near-race-rep levels of performance and all-around usability. Honda’s reputation for refinement is no more exemplified than on the VFR, its liquid-smooth powerband a testament to the simple beauty of a cable-actuated throttle and a perfectly tuned carburetor.</p><p>Be that as it may, we can’t help but wonder, “What if the Interceptor had continued as Big Red’s sportbike platform?” And, can we please have a modern version of the RC36-2?</p><p>1993 Honda CBR900RR</p><img alt=\"The best part of the 1990s: the sportbikes.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/T_Eemi7s035X99Zgtwnip1CNEnU=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/G4OPBLKKONFQBPHZKEV76ZLFOI.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>The best part of the 1990s: the sportbikes. (Honda/)</caption><p>It’s no stretch to say the <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/blogs/1993-honda-cbr900rr-bike-changed-my-life/\" target=\"_blank\">1993 Honda CBR900RR</a> was a big factor in starting the liter-class wars of the following decade. At the time, liter-class motorcycles were more GT bikes than track weapons—think Kawasaki ZX-11. So when the CBR came out in 1993, weighing 144 pounds less than the ZX-11 (yes, you read that correctly), we should have known everything was about to change.</p><p>Since then, the CBR has been a mainstay in Honda’s lineup even while other bikes, like the RC51, made racetrack headlines. Tadao Baba’s original Blade is still one of the most iconic sportbikes of all time.</p><p>1990 Honda Hawk GT NT650</p><img alt=\"In Japan, the NT650 carried the model name “Bros,” not Hawk GT. There’s probably a joke in there somewhere.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/wkrxThYuUaQWLelRFHSr9VWy7iQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/OFBOVT2Y45A27B6TGF3LKXTQLI.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>In Japan, the NT650 carried the model name “Bros,” not Hawk GT. There’s probably a joke in there somewhere. (Honda/)</caption><p>Imagine if Honda unveiled a 647cc V-twin naked bike with a racy twin-spar aluminum frame and a trick single-sided swingarm designed by <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/elf-racing-elf-motorcycle-chassis-designs-retrospective/\" target=\"_blank\">ELF</a> at EICMA in 2021. It’d be the talk of the show. You’d want it. We’d all want it.</p><p>The thing is, Honda built this very thing from 1988–1990. The NT650, or Hawk GT, wasn’t a hot seller. But now it’s a cult classic. It’s greyhound-like litheness makes the SV650 look like a barrel-shaped labrador retriever.</p><p>The Hawk GT is kind of a greatest-hits album of motorcycling must-haves. It’s undeniably cool yet has a utilitarian vibe, it’s light and sporty, it’s practically bulletproof. There’s just something about it. Something universal. The Hawk is so appealing and affordable that some people own several at once.</p><p>1989 Honda GB500</p><img alt=\"Honda’s answer to the “classic 500cc British single,” the GB500 Tourist Trophy.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"393\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/2loDt-9PMuDSrysBVwd9DjqiTfc=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/5YI4RI2RMZA7TJ7GUNBHZWAGHE.jpg\" width=\"590\"/><br/><caption>Honda’s answer to the “classic 500cc British single,” the GB500 Tourist Trophy. (Honda/)</caption><p>Honda’s interpretation of the classic 500cc British single, the GB500 Tourist Trophy is another cult classic. Built from 1989–1990, the GB never sold well. First, it was a single, and Americans haven’t been too keen on singles since, well, somebody put two cylinders in a frame. Secondly, 30 years ago, classic café racer styling wasn’t as en vogue as it is today. So what we have here is an ahead-of-its time motorcycle based on an old-fashioned-style motorcycle that was never immensely popular with Americans in the first place. In other words, a marketer’s nightmare.</p><p>Anyway, the GB500′s air-cooled mill was derived from the 1983 XR500 dirt bike, and sported a single 42mm round-slide Keihin carburetor. It also had preload-adjustable Showa suspension, electric-start and a kickstarter, and a single disc brake up front. And it weighed less than 400 pounds fully fueled. What’s not to like, America?</p><p>It may not be a real-deal Velocette or BSA, but it is a classic in its own right. Then again, Honda didn’t build the GB thinking it ever would be, you’d imagine. It’s classic for the very fact that it _isn’t_ an old British single, but a motorcycle built in Japan in the ’80s by people who romanticized a glorious era before their own. In other words, it was an idea that didn’t catch on until decades later. Classic Honda.</p><p>1983 Honda VF750F Interceptor</p><img alt=\"Along with the first-gen Suzuki GSX-R750, the Honda Interceptor, in many ways, is the first iteration of the modern sportbike.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/u7ZzRahwr-5E-pS3LxMYHKDAP3A=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/JIRMRFQ6I5GPLLVRV6IN2K6VJE.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>Along with the first-gen Suzuki GSX-R750, the Honda Interceptor, in many ways, is the first iteration of the modern sportbike. (Honda/)</caption><p>Honda has long been synonymous with four-stroke V-4s, thanks in large part to the groundbreaking 1983 Honda Interceptor, one of the first production motorcycles, along with the 1985 Suzuki GSX-R750, to create the sportbike category as we know it. Americans, common thinking of the time went, only cared about straight-line performance. The Interceptor either proved otherwise, or gave us a reason to fall in love with corners. It wasn’t long before consumers went sportbike mad—ironic, considering Honda’s primary objective for the Interceptor was racetrack success.</p><p>For the 1983 season, the AMA decreased the displacement limit in the superbike class from 1,000cc to 750cc with the hope of remedying the sphincter-testing handling qualities of the era’s brute-ish superbikes. While the Interceptor won six straight races in ’83, Wayne Rainey won the championship aboard a Muzzy Kawasaki GPz750. Then Honda went on to win the next five championships.</p><p>1979 Honda CBX1000</p><img alt=\"Look at all those header pipes!\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/-qVVruYFHL61bSGtgvCx_CFPOyk=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/FM7VUGBYI5H53OQYIOLNA2XEEY.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>Look at all those header pipes! (Honda/)</caption><p>The 1979 <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/1970s-motorcycles-hondas-six-cylinder-cbx/\" target=\"_blank\">CBX seems to represent broader cultural tastes</a> than it does the motorcycling world’s own development trends. Consider: the Interceptor’s debut was only four years away, and the “light means right” mantra not far behind it. The CBX wasn’t a sportbike per se. It wasn’t a Gold Wing either. It was just…cool. The way its 1,047cc engine dangles beneath the frame sans downtubes, giving an uninterrupted view of its six glorious chromed header pipes accentuates its six-iness. The CBX is a double-take bike because it almost looks wrong. So, so wrong.</p><p>The CBX was designed by Soichiro Irimajiri, who was responsible for the now-legendary six-cylinder RC165 and RC166 grand prix racers. You can almost hear the Honda marketing men saying, “And if a little magic happens to rub off from Hailwood’s machines, then so be it.” And that association is just about where the CBX’s racing credentials end. While Honda notoriously added cylinders to its racebikes in the ’60s in search of more revs and more horsepower, the CBX revved to a conservative 9,000 rpm and produced 105 hp. Wet weight was a burly 600 pounds.</p><p>The CBX persists in giving the impression that Honda built it just because it could. It didn’t impact the motorcycling world like the Gold Wing or Interceptor did, but here we are still talking about it. And mentioning the RC166 in the same breath.</p><p>1969 Honda CB750</p><img alt=\"The motorcycle that changed the course of the sport forever.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1125\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/g_n69-91IF6uCyaYmuPPa2UzCRg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/TXMPIF4FRVGNNDN6PAZNRE3P2U.jpg\" width=\"1500\"/><br/><caption>The motorcycle that changed the course of the sport forever. (Honda/)</caption><p>The <a href=\"https://www.motorcyclistonline.com/how-to/1969-1978-honda-cb750-smart-money/\" target=\"_blank\">CB750</a> can be considered both a fitting end and a rapturous beginning. Taking into account all Honda’s success in grand prix racing with multicylinder machines and its reputation for mass-producing reliable, affordable small-displacement single- and twin-cylinder motorcycles, the CB750 seemed to be the culmination of Honda’s decades-long work. At the same time, it triggered the beginning of a new era in which Japanese four-cylinders became the prevalent performance-oriented motorcycle.</p><p>The CB750 changed the motorcycle world.</p><p>There are plenty of other models that could fit on this list, like the CB400 or the XR650L. What other models do you think could make the cut? Comment below.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Sat, 29 Aug 2020 21:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/3s8Ljf5FNfwVRkbBFkD-izi6J0c=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/C7ZXMYOFIVFY5IJOX2MW6EI4M4.jpg","linkMd5":"af9f44feb934affed4f0a3b43bd56a1c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn51@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-06-55-285_06bdd727f361c822.webp","destWidth":1500,"destHeight":1125,"sourceBytes":260667,"destBytes":234668,"author":"By -us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/JIRMRFQ6I5GPLLVRV6IN2K6VJE.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn100@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-03-173_21377056f9b41137.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/-qVVruYFHL61bSGtgvCx_CFPOyk=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/FM7VUGBYI5H53OQYIOLNA2XEEY.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn28@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-04-491_6a3db8e4ff0a55a8.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/g_n69-91IF6uCyaYmuPPa2UzCRg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/TXMPIF4FRVGNNDN6PAZNRE3P2U.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn92@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-07-03-302_68ad7f29c08a610b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394623},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"How cats and dogs see the world","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/science/cat-dog-human-vision-color-blind/","description":"<img alt=\"What can dogs see?\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"880\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/dbCIPEe3vHrKsi2E4tMwSexVgfo=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/VPJZX6TT4JFCRCD66X2XXBRJ3Q.jpg\" width=\"1320\"/><br/><caption>What can dogs see? (Stan Horaczek /)</caption><p><a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/tags/pet-psychic/\"><img alt=\"Pet Psychic Banner\" src=\"https://cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/COUYOTHJ5JGLXJJFJA7ZS7ZQ4Y.jpg\" style=\"width:100%\"/></a></p><p><i>Ever wish you could peer into your cat, dog, skink, or betta fish’s brain? It would give you a far better perspective of the world—or at least help you be a smarter pet parent. We’re here to demystify your animals (to some extent), while also shedding advice on how you can best thrive together. Welcome to Pet Psychic.</i></p><p>Take a quick flash picture of your dog or cat and their eyes will light up in a way that’s equal parts cool and unnerving. That eerie glow comes from a layer of their eyes called a tapetum and it reflects light to let our furry pals see small amounts of light much more efficiently than our human eyes. In fact, our pets sense the world we live in much differently than people do.</p><p>As children, many of us learned that cats and dogs are “colorblind” or see the world in “black and white”—but <a href=\"https://www.akc.org/expert-advice/health/are-dogs-color-blind/\" target=\"_blank\">that’s not entirely true</a>. “They [just] can’t see all the different colors that we can see,” says Katherine Houpt, a professor at Cornell’s College of Veterinary Medicine . “From what we can tell, they see the world in shades of blue and yellow.”</p><img alt=\"This demonstration is purely for color purposes. The yellow and blue tones come through, but reds don't. In reality, the dog and cat vision would also be a lot blurrier.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1531\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/d7OG0DI-uOydh6JjP4GEL64L-tI=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/RIP2OWJJORGJFAILTUC23OE6KA.jpg\" width=\"2041\"/><br/><caption>This demonstration is purely for color purposes. The yellow and blue tones come through, but reds don't. In reality, the dog and cat vision would also be a lot blurrier. (Stan Horaczek /)</caption><p>From a physiology standpoint, the unique view comes down to types of light receptors in the eye itself. “Because dogs and cats are predators, they don’t have to be able to tell the difference between some similar shades,” Houpt explains. “As primates, we have to know whether that persimmon is ripe or not. We’re better at color discrimination in order to find the correct foods.” In other words, a grey rabbit is just as tasty as a brown one.</p><p>When it comes to clarity, humans also have an advantage over our domesticated pals. If a dog can make out an object from 20 feet away, a human can see it from 60 feet. The difference is even more pronounced for cats—what a cat can see from 20 feet, a human can see from 100 or even 200 feet out. Our pets aren’t built to process crystal clear images of the world around them.</p><p>The animals do have an advantage, however, when it comes to perceiving movement—a skill honed to help them catch their fast-moving food. While most cats and dogs have trouble spotting still objects very close to them, they can spot movement for up to a half-mile—even if it’s relatively subtle.</p><p>They can also see more at once than humans. While our field of view is approximately 180 degrees, feline and canine eyes bulge out a little to expand their peripheral vision <a href=\"https://www.sagecenters.com/veterinaryvision/resources/what-do-dogs-and-cats-see/#:~:text=Dogs+have+eyes+which+are,half+that+possessed+by+humans.\" target=\"_blank\">past 200 degrees</a>. That same motion detection extends to the edges of their vision, so even if they can’t tell what’s moving, they know something’s coming.</p><p>That field of view can differ  depending on the breed of dog, though. A bulldog, for instance, doesn’t have to see around a giant snout like some other breeds, which comes in handy when observing objects up close and gives them a bigger overall field of unobstructed view.</p><p>One commonly held conception that’s true: When it comes to seeing in the dark, cat and dog eyes excel, in part because the tapetum reflects illumination to the light receptors. “They can’t see in absolute darkness any better than we can,” says Houpt. “But in dim light, they can see better.” That’s because their eyes contain more rods than ours—between six and eight times more for cats—which means they require less illumination to collect a usable picture for their brain.</p><p>But their uncanny perception doesn’t end there. Once you’ve considered all of the advantages and shortcomings of cat and dog vision, you still have to consider how it interplays with their other senses.</p><p>Cats can hear <a href=\"https://www.google.com/search?q=cats+hear+ultrasonic+sound&#38;rlz=1C5CHFA_enUS876US876&#38;oq=cats+hear+ultrasonic+sound&#38;aqs=chrome..69i57j0l2.3742j0j7&#38;sourceid=chrome&#38;ie=UTF-8\" target=\"_blank\">ultrasonic pitches</a> typically emitted by their prey, which allows them to continue the chase, even if their scurrying snack gets out of eye shot. They also use smell as a primary method to identify people. “When you take a cat to the vet, your second cat may not recognize it because it smells like other people. And if you’ve been gone for a while and come back, the cat may not recognize you until it takes a good whiff,” Houpt says. “If you changed shampoos while you were gone, you might be in for a problem.”</p><p>Dogs obviously have a keen sense of smell as well. Like cats and other animals, they have a <a href=\"https://vcahospitals.com/know-your-pet/how-dogs-use-smell-to-perceive-the-world#:~:text=Unlike+humans,+dogs+have+an,mouth+behind+the+upper+incisors.\" target=\"_blank\">vomeronasal organ</a> as part of the olfactory system specifically dedicated to their olfactory needs. It allows wilder things to communicate via scentand improves the overall sense of smell. Depending on the breed, a dog’s sense of smell ranges from 1,000 and 10,000 times more effective than a human’s.</p><p>With those extra tools at their disposal, dogs and cats don’t need to depend on their eyes to navigate the world. Their visual limitations, however, can provide some interesting problems for humans trying to teach animals new tricks. “Because of how they see color, dogs are not very good at traffic lights,” Houpt says. “That becomes a problem when you’re teaching a guide dog so they have to learn that the darker yellow is when they have to stop.” In an ideal world, traffic lights would have an olfactory component so helpful pooches could smell when it’s time to go.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 28 Aug 2020 21:03:15 nightly routine. (Matheus Ferrero via Unsplash/)</caption><p>If you’ve had braces, you likely know about water flossers. These dental care appliances shoot a pinpoint jet of water at teeth to dislodge any food particles—particularly around the wires and brackets of orthodontic appliances. But they’re also useful tools for everyone else, cleaning teeth while massaging gums to promote good oral health. They’re also ideal for people who, because of reduced manual dexterity, might not be able to use traditional string floss.</p><p>Many water flossers are counter-top units, with large reservoirs for water and multiple power settings. However, some come in portable models, perfect for throwing into a suitcase when on the go.</p><p>While water flossers can’t get into all the narrow spaces between teeth like string floss can, they do cover a larger surface area and, combined with flossing and brushing, help provide a complete cleaning for that fresh-from-the-dentist feeling.</p><img alt=\"When you want to treat your mouth to the best.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/vFXbyL41Ejr4NpVnwr58411RNko=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/DNTPMFWT4JDEXKZO6Z5NFI2BBQ.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>When you want to treat your mouth to the best. The irrigator rotates a full 360 degrees to reach all your teeth, and a helpful timer beeps every 30 seconds as a reminder to shift to a different part of your mouth. The settings are controlled by a dial on the main unit, and on/off switches are located on the flosser’s handle and on the Aquarius’s base. Panasonic’s unit runs on two AA batteries, making it perfect for any place an outlet might not be available for charging, like camping, or when stuck at an airport.</p><img alt=\"Colorful design.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/IMiPd3pJdc6MuwTLx0HliL_K4ZM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/QGIO6SXTARGMTEEK7Z3HDYR7OE.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>Colorful design. Team","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/5l0gwoRxKLMksNKsGYSiSZbuv88=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/QU3Q47TAABASBEOR2ILLNLWJI4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn7@2020_6/2020/08/31/05-06-54-653_bd9b3afcc720ad1f.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/vFXbyL41Ejr4NpVnwr58411RNko=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/DNTPMFWT4JDEXKZO6Z5NFI2BBQ.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn39@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-01-814_241b584b5a529674.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/kJHpBxhTuu359sgVd-9fiQ46ACI=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/GD6TYNHYZVAFTIDXWUTASVT2XI.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn91@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-07-01-857_94a4aa104b217bd2.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/EBX1G5vsOWkzxevsB6BA8Fb0VQQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/MMUK6C45FFEQLH5IFO6WJWRBEM.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn96@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-02-070_5951ffb2661bc3f7.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/IMiPd3pJdc6MuwTLx0HliL_K4ZM=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/QGIO6SXTARGMTEEK7Z3HDYR7OE.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn80@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-07-03-617_4f672bd927fbb018.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394626},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"The right way to walk your dog","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/best-walk-dog-guide-tips/","description":"<img alt=\"A nice, relaxing stroll.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/mdtsAqUOKVHkUaMmVp8usHn5nEA=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/GVGGK5MCDBEP3PS22LJMHY7HV4.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>A nice, relaxing stroll. (Stephen Goldberg/Unsplash/)</caption><p><a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/tags/pet-psychic/\"><img alt=\"Pet Psychic Banner\" src=\"https://cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/COUYOTHJ5JGLXJJFJA7ZS7ZQ4Y.jpg\" style=\"width:100%\"/></a></p><p><i>Ever wish you could peer into your cat, dog, skink, or betta fish’s brain? It would give you a far better perspective of the world—or at least help you be a smarter pet parent. We’re here to demystify your animals (to some extent), while also shedding advice on how you can best thrive together. Welcome to Pet Psychic.</i></p><p>Walking your dog seems simple enough—it’s just you, your pup, and the great outdoors. But without the right equipment and approach, a much-anticipated W-A-L-K can be disappointing.</p><p>Get some good gear</p><p>Search for “walking a dog” online and you’ll find pages of photos with people holding leashes attached to a collar around a dog’s throat, which is exactly what the experts we spoke to advise against.</p><p>“I’m not a big fan of anything around the neck that’s pulling,” says Anna Mynchenberg, a manager at <a href=\"https://bark.co/\" target=\"_blank\">Bark</a>, the company behind BarkBox. “I can’t imagine having any pressure on my neck, so I wouldn’t want to do that to my dog.”</p><p>There are simply too many reasons your furry best friend might pull on the leash, pressing the collar against their windpipe, says Kate Perry, <a href=\"https://kateperrydogtraining.com/\" target=\"_blank\">a dog trainer</a> based in New York City. That could choke them or, in a worst-case scenario where they leap off a bridge, leave them dangling by their neck.</p><p>A comfortable harness</p><p>You can avoid potentially strangling your dog by buying a harness. There are many to choose from, including models that can help <a href=\"https://www.rover.com/blog/no-pull-dog-harnesses-that-work-your-complete-guide-to-the-best-options/\" target=\"_blank\">keep an eager canine from pulling</a> on the leash. Mynchenberg, who has a master’s degree in human-canine life sciences from <a href=\"https://www.berginu.edu/\" target=\"_blank\">Bergin University of Canine Studies</a>, says she likes <a href=\"https://amzn.to/32kQlu6\">harnesses with handles</a> because they offer an easy way to grab and hold your pet.</p><p>Going a little deeper, if your dog is stronger than you, or if you’re training a puppy, consider <a href=\"https://amzn.to/3ljKNJa\" target=\"_blank\">a front-clip</a> or <a href=\"https://amzn.to/2FYyiT3\" target=\"_blank\">no-pull harness</a>. Leashes that attach at the back of a dog’s shoulders only reinforce pulling, Perry says. But fasten one to the front, and it’ll block much of your pup’s power and keep them from yanking, she explains.</p><p>Before you strap on a harness, though, Perry recommends taking a few days to familiarize your dog with the equipment, as they may not be comfortable with it at first. That means creating positive associations by putting it on and taking it off multiple times in a row while giving them treats, and buckling them in during meals and playtime.</p><p>A leash that lets you maintain control</p><p>For most, if not all, of the time you’re walking your dog, your hand will be holding a leash—so get something comfortable. “You want something you can really grip,” Perry says. For a medium-size dog, she suggests a ½- to ¾-inch-wide nylon leash. Try <a href=\"https://amzn.to/2QlXiFM\" target=\"_blank\">rope-style leashes</a> for another option.</p><p>Perry’s ideal leash is 6 feet long with three sets of knots along the length to use as grips if your dog pulls away from you (also the perfect length to help you keep proper social distance from anyone you walk by). If you don’t want to knot up your lead, you can buy a <a href=\"https://amzn.to/2CZURFH\" target=\"_blank\">leash</a> that has two or <a href=\"https://amzn.to/2QlXHIi\" target=\"_blank\">three loops</a> sewn in.</p><p>A <a href=\"https://amzn.to/3j93ygi\" target=\"_blank\">waist leash</a> is another option. Mynchenberg says she likes these because they allow her to keep her hands free to work on training or simply enjoy time with her dog. Perry, however, isn’t a fan of these, as your dog could pull and injure you. Ultimately, it comes down to how well you know your dog and how well you’ve trained them, she says.</p><p>Another note: Don’t get a retractable leash unless you’re going to spend a lot of time roaming parks, beaches, or hiking trails, where you can let your dog run relatively free. On city streets, they can be more trouble than they’re worth. For one, you don’t really have control when you let the leash spool out, and while you’re supposed to be able to reel it back in, they sometimes malfunction. Perry has also seen unsuspecting pedestrians walk into leashes linking dogs and owners that are far apart.</p><p>Other useful options</p><p>A harness and leash are the bare minimum you need to walk a dog, but you should bring <a href=\"https://amzn.to/3jefBcg\" target=\"_blank\">a bunch of bags</a> along to pick up your pup’s poop, too. Don’t forget treats, either, especially if your dog is highly motivated by snacks, like Mynchenberg’s two boxers, Hercules and Denver. “If they want that squirrel, you have to have something better,” she says.</p><p>And if you like to accessorize, <a href=\"https://www.wsj.com/articles/in-the-covid-era-purses-are-out-fanny-packs-are-in-11597831215\" target=\"_blank\">consider a fanny pack</a>. They can hold treats, your phone, your keys, and whatever else you normally would’ve shoved into your pockets or carried in your hands, Mynchenberg says.</p><p>Plan when to walk your dog</p><img alt=\"A misty morning walk will keep your dog cool.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1338\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/bpvXSz00wgLErEVN7g-xjNUiOlg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/N2WHZ6LWBRDXHMVNGKBYRHME2Q.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>A misty morning walk will keep your dog cool. (Tadeusz Lakota/Unsplash/)</caption><p>Depending on your dog and the reason for walking, you should hit the street one to four times a day. That could include one long exercise trek, two shorter workouts, and/or bathroom time. Mynchenberg suggests heading out in the morning or evening to avoid midday heat and traveling less-crowded paths to avoid overstimulating your pup. These numbers may vary depending on your pet’s age and energy level, as well as whether you have a fenced-in yard.</p><p>Keep things consistent</p><p>As soon as the leash is on, decide which side you will keep the dog on for the duration of the walk. “If I’m walking with a child, I won’t let them zig-zag around, but will hold them in my left or right hand,” Perry explains. “You’re loving your dog by giving it some order.” Stop to reposition them if they wander astray while you’re moving. It’s also important that if multiple people are taking the dog out, they all maintain the same basic procedures, Perry says.</p><p>Think about the route</p><p>In short, don’t walk your dog on hot surfaces. If you can’t touch the ground with your bare hand or step on it with bare feet, it’ll hurt your pet, Perry and Mynchenberg both say.</p><p>Beyond that, though, the preferred path depends on the dog. Among Mynchenberg’s companions, Denver will walk on anything because he’s had a lot of experience getting to know different environments. Hercules, on the other hand, took a long time to walk over bridges. If something is new, dogs sometimes respond with fear or hesitation—so if they’re not a fan of what’s around them, you’ll know, she says.</p><p>Pick up your dog’s poop</p><p>That’s it. That’s the tip. Don’t let it decompose in nature or “fertilize” someone’s yard. Send it straight to the landfill.</p><p>Keep your dog’s pee away from people</p><p>Generally, try to find a grassy area or curb for your dog to urinate on and stick to it. Ideally, you’ll want to train your dog to pee in one or a few spots and keep them away from hosing down anything people might touch or pick up, including trash cans, Mynchenberg says.</p><p>Learn to handle distractions</p><p>If your <a href=\"https://www.dogster.com/lifestyle/dog-training-strong-prey-drive\" target=\"_blank\">dog has a high prey drive</a>, anything that moves quickly will spark excitement, Mynchenberg says. Having the right equipment is one part of the solution, but training is the other. For example, Mychenberg’s dog Hercules will react to people and other canines, so they taught him to heel to her right side. He knows this means treats, pets, and praise, so he responds to the command well, she says.</p><p>You should also train your dog to look back at you every time they see a squirrel or something interesting, to essentially ask permission to investigate. Whenever your pooch does so, you’ll want to reward it, Mynchenberg says. She explains that every time Hercules sees a person, he checks in with her.</p><p>If your dog isn’t well-trained yet, it’s important that you understand their triggers and how much they can handle stimulus-wise. Know what to look for and keep an eye on your surroundings so you can manage any potential problems before the situation escalates, Perry says. Cities like the one she lives in are full of distractions, so she likes to stand between two parked cars and do command training to get the dog she’s with to focus on her and ignore whatever’s whizzing by.</p><p>Don’t always follow the same route</p><p>Once you’ve established a solid walking regimen, it’s good to keep things interesting. “Dogs will find something to sniff even if the path is familiar, but it adds that extra layer of excitement if you go somewhere new,” Mynchenberg says. If you’re still training your dog, however, stick to a route your pup is used to, which limits distractions.</p><p>Understand the risks of exercising with your dog</p><p>You may be a marathoner or an expert cyclist, but your dog may not be (we’ll bet they can’t even ride a bike). “Just like humans have to work up to running or exercising, dogs do, too,” Mynchenberg says. “You’re not going to be setting any personal records on your dog’s first run.”</p><p>If you do exercise with your dog, pay attention to them and make sure you meet their needs first. That means running when it’s cool out, bringing a bottle of water to keep them hydrated, and taking frequent breaks. After all, <a href=\"https://vcahospitals.com/know-your-pet/taking-your-pets-temperature\" target=\"_blank\">a dog’s normal body temperature</a> is a few degrees higher than a human’s, and they’ve got a full suit of hair. “Imagine having a fever, with a coat on, and running,” Perry says.</p><p>Riding a bike with a dog is more complicated because wheels move faster than legs (even four of them). Any ride you take will have to be fairly leisurely. There’s also the risk that your dog pulls the bike off balance. But before you even get on a bike, you’ll want to make sure your dog is used to being near them. A good way to familiarize them with the machine, Mychenberg says, is to walk them next to it. To avoid having to hold anything while riding, you can get <a href=\"https://amzn.to/3aXOPlJ\" target=\"_blank\">a bike seat attachment</a> that connects to your dog’s leash.</p><p>Be careful when going off-leash</p><p>Before you set your dog free, make sure you know the leash laws in your area. Many places require dogs to remain linked to their humans at all times. You’ll also want to have rock-solid commands that will make them come to your side immediately, stop what they’re doing, and drop animals and objects they’ve picked up, Perry says.</p><p>Both Perry and Mynchenberg stress that enclosed areas, like a dog park or fenced-in yard, are most ideal for off-leash sessions. That goes for well-trained dogs, too, because once you take the leash off, a lot of factors will be beyond your control. Even wide open areas like beaches and parks are risky because your dog may encounter an unexpected trigger or unseen danger, says Perry, who has also authored the book <i>Training For Both Ends of the Leash</i>. In such situations, it’s crucial to have a deep understanding of your pet and a learned list of commands.</p><p>Cool down after the walk</p><p>Once you get back home, you can flop down and relax or try to squeeze a little more productivity out of your pooch. When a dog is calm and tired post-walk, Mynchenberg likes to take advantage of that time to work on essentials that are tough when a dog is amped up, like crate training.</p><p>Not every walk is going to be perfect, but if you have a plan and know what you and your dog want to get out of it before you go, each one will be better than the last.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 26 Aug 2020 19:01:27 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/mdtsAqUOKVHkUaMmVp8usHn5nEA=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/GVGGK5MCDBEP3PS22LJMHY7HV4.jpg","linkMd5":"274e1adb0b83e97aba6218efbd0b8436","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn90@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-06-55-563_47b7b36c7a78c916.webp","destWidth":2000,"destHeight":1500,"sourceBytes":623055,"destBytes":478534,"author":"John Kennedy","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/mdtsAqUOKVHkUaMmVp8usHn5nEA=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/GVGGK5MCDBEP3PS22LJMHY7HV4.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn90@2020_5/2020/08/31/05-06-55-563_47b7b36c7a78c916.webp","https://cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/COUYOTHJ5JGLXJJFJA7ZS7ZQ4Y.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn31@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-03-396_dd3a5be32b6985b7.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/bpvXSz00wgLErEVN7g-xjNUiOlg=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/N2WHZ6LWBRDXHMVNGKBYRHME2Q.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn66@2020_3/2020/08/31/05-07-03-101_f6fe80d002e34b75.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394632},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Five cool ways to upcycle old coffee sacks","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/coffee-sack-diy-projects/","description":"<img alt=\"You see burlap, we see couture.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/1gq0REKo--YCDSHsBFvYmxFKjWQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/ZR4N4MOQA5CBHISTWXH55CDLHA.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>You see burlap, we see couture. ( Thomas Thompson / Unsplash/)</caption><p>Fresh coffee goes stale too fast to travel far. The beans in that daily espresso you get from your local shop were probably roasted just a few miles down the road—or even in the shop itself.</p><p>Your local independent roaster is probably buying big bags of raw coffee from Asia, South America, or Africa, and the burlap bags it comes in—<a href=\"http://www.ico.org/\" target=\"_blank\">produced by the millions each year</a>—are mostly a waste product. Unless someone does something with them.</p><p>Where to find coffee sacks</p><p>The simplest way is to ask at a local café that roasts their own beans. Each sack makes around 700 standard cups of coffee, if you were wondering—so even micro-roasters go through a couple of them a week.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.portlandcoffeeroasters.com/about/sustainability/\" target=\"_blank\">Some roasters</a> will happily give you a few sacks for free, especially if you’re a customer. Other slightly more enterprising cafés will charge a couple of bucks for each one or<a href=\"https://www.deargreencoffee.com/products/used-coffee-sacks\" target=\"_blank\"> ask for a donation to charity</a>. As a ballpark, the standard price sits somewhere around $1 and $2 per sack—I paid less than $10 to have four of them shipped to my door, so if you’re spending more than that you’ll probably want to check other shops for a better deal.</p><p>If you’re visiting your roaster in person, you’ll likely be able to pick out whatever sacks you want, but if you buy them online, you’ll probably just get a random assortment. If you need to calculate a specific amount of raw material for a project, don’t worry—<a href=\"http://coffeetime.wikidot.com/standard-coffee-sack-weights-green-coffee\" target=\"_blank\">the size is pretty standard</a> (about 38 by 26 inches) so the biggest difference will be the designs printed on the front.</p><p>How to clean a jute sack</p><p>By the time you get them, your coffee sacks will have travelled halfway around the world, if not further, so don’t be surprised if they are hard-used, wrinkled, and a bit dirty. Depending on what you want to do with them, you’ll likely want to give them a wash first.</p><p>The loose hessian weave of most coffee sacks won’t survive the washer and dryer, so the best thing to do is hand-wash them in soapy water and leave them to hang dry.</p><p>To eliminate any wrinkles, grab an iron and work out any creases on the highest setting—it’s not a light fabric so it can take a few passes.</p><p>What to do with coffee sacks</p><img alt=\"You can use a coffee sack to make a tote bag or a planter. Or a tote that doubles as a planter.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"1500\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/GL--BH6YNz4wcEDSMc4l7ac7ddw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/NELE77BQ6NGIPNMKCFWITKXMPM.jpg\" width=\"2000\"/><br/><caption>You can use a coffee sack to make a tote bag or a planter. Or a tote that doubles as a planter. (Priscilla Du Preez / Unsplash/)</caption><p>Coffee sacks are a great source of burlap, which you can use for <a href=\"https://youtu.be/-nC1bJBhjew?t=13\" target=\"_blank\">pretty much anything you can imagine</a>. But they don’t just have to be raw material.</p><p>Turn them into grow sacks</p><p>Burlap is seriously useful in the garden. The hardy material can stand up to the weather while still letting your plants drain. “Gangsta gardener” and master of repurposing, <a href=\"http://ronfinley.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Ron Finley</a>, suggests<a href=\"https://www.masterclass.com/articles/ron-finley-how-to-grow-potatoes-in-a-sack-like-a-gangsta\" target=\"_blank\"> growing potatoes in them</a>, but he<a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/p/CAD1DIfBSuc/\" target=\"_blank\"> also uses them as a base material in his planter boxes</a>. My girlfriend and I liked how they looked so much, we used them to plant flowers we later put outside the front door.</p><p>Build a pet bed</p><p>If you want a quick and easy pet bed, it’s hard to beat a coffee sack. Take a clean one, stuff it with a pillow, put it on the ground, and watch your pets flock to it. If you want to take things further, you can<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=teoK1ooITsM\" target=\"_blank\"> sew a zipper</a> to the open side of the sack to seal it up.</p><p>Craft a custom tote bag </p><p>You can use a coffee sack as the base for <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I29nGw4w6dw\" target=\"_blank\">any fabric-project you like</a>, but <a href=\"https://www.popsci.com/story/diy/build-sew-zipper-tote-bag/\" target=\"_blank\">a tote bag</a> might be the simplest. After all, you’re mostly just adding handles. Depending on your level of skill (and access to a sewing machine), you can make <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ae5HdofvviY\" target=\"_blank\">a basic tote</a> or go all in and<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=daB8YUPsgTo\" target=\"_blank\"> create a gorgeous beach bag</a>. Just make sure to wash your sack first.</p><p>Create some decent decor</p><p>Coffee bags are marked with the details of their contents and origin. Some of the designs look pretty cool, especially when cleaned up. Just wash, press, and frame a sack to add a special touch to any room in your house. You’ll have to trim a bit off the edges, but a <a href=\"https://amzn.to/3gsXJZl\" target=\"_blank\">36-by-24-inch frame</a> will give you the best results.</p><p>Organize a sack race</p><p>This was my best event on sports day, so I’d back myself in a sack race over anyone—even Usain Bolt. With a couple of spare coffee sacks lying about, you can replace your routine workout with a fun and wholesome race. Find someone to compete with and let the jumping begin.</p>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 27 Aug 2020 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":410,"bgimg":"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/1gq0REKo--YCDSHsBFvYmxFKjWQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/ZR4N4MOQA5CBHISTWXH55CDLHA.jpg","linkMd5":"91ece2007df3605a6314ffc0701e1984","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn81@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-06-35-316_3c6ab6bb9f2d505d.webp","destWidth":2000,"destHeight":1500,"sourceBytes":238447,"destBytes":151848,"author":"Harry Guinness","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/1gq0REKo--YCDSHsBFvYmxFKjWQ=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/ZR4N4MOQA5CBHISTWXH55CDLHA.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn81@2020_4/2020/08/31/05-06-35-316_3c6ab6bb9f2d505d.webp","https://www.popsci.com/resizer/GL--BH6YNz4wcEDSMc4l7ac7ddw=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/NELE77BQ6NGIPNMKCFWITKXMPM.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn91@2020_2/2020/08/31/05-07-02-046_b44f8b08d66285e9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598850394633},{"createdTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-31 13:06:34","title":"Backpacks that will charge your phone ","link":"https://www.popsci.com/story/shop/backpacks-that-charge-your-phone/","description":"<img alt=\"Always be prepared.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"625\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/lClRybGGDzETIV3G0_aUKX_XYCU=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/SXHWIG7CJRDENNS7LBFFLCZ6WE.jpg\" width=\"938\"/><br/><caption>Always be prepared. (Felix Rostig via Unsplash /)</caption><p>Is there anywhere you want to go without your phone these days? Probably not. Whether you’re hiking a trail, packing for the beach, commuting to work, or on the way to class you’re going to want to keep your phone charged. These nifty backpacks come with built-in batteries to charge your phone while offering the storage capacity and durability of regular backpacks.</p><img alt=\"You’ve got the glow.\" data-has-syndication-rights=\"1\" height=\"626\" src=\"https://www.popsci.com/resizer/FIIHkBkrISVfiCzbsmW0gHWJbKI=/cloudfront-us-east-1.images.arcpublishing.com/bonnier/ABLYKTRNRFAS5D2NYWS5EUBA3A.jpg\" width=\"1000\"/><br/><caption>You’ve got the glow.
